It is not of atomic magnitude, because, the experience<br>of pleasure etc is felt all over the body; nor is it of any<br>intermediate magnitude, for, if so, the soul would be liable<br>to destruction in which case the assumption that every action<br>the soul performs during its sojourn on earth will perish<br>without yielding its fruit and that it will enjoy the fruits<br>of deeds which it has not performed, becomes peremptory.<br>Therefore, the individual soul is eternal and all-pervading.<br>[41. The syllogism in this instance is as follows: 'Every effect<br>is produced by a causal agent, eg a pot; A dyad which is the<br>least component of the element Earth is an effect; Therefore it is<br>produced by a Causal Agent.' The major premise here is supported<br>by such instances as a pot, cloth etc. And there is no instance<br>to prove the contrary. The premise therefore is quite logical. This<br>Causal Agent can be only God as no human or sub-human being<br>can possibly be an agent in the production of a dyad which is<br>formed of two atoms inasmuch as atoms are not perceived by any<br>human or sub-human agent. Thus, the existence of a super-human<br>agent has to be postulated to explain the creation of the world<br>and that is proof enough for the existence of God.<br>The number of dyads is infinite; it, therefore, follows that only an omniscient soul can have an immediate apprehension of the infinite number of atoms of all the Elements. Thus the causal agent is the immediate knower of everything which again proves the existence of Iśvara.]
        - **Item 18**:
          - **verse_number**: `18`
          - **original_text**: 9. Manas (Mind):<br>सुखाद्युपलब्धिसाधनमिन्द्रियं मनः ।<br>तच्च प्रत्यात्मनियतत्वादनन्तं परमाणुरूपं नित्यं च ॥ ९ ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 9. Mind is the (internal) organ which is the instrument<br>in obtaining the knowledge of pleasure etc. It, being<br>linked up with each human soul, is innumerable, atomic<br>and eternal.
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: मनसो लक्षणमाह । सुखेति । स्पर्शरहितत्वे सति क्रियावत्वं मनसो लक्षणम् ।<br>तद्विभजते । तच्चेति । एकैकस्यात्मन एकैकं मन इत्यात्मनामनेकत्वान्<br>मसोऽप्यनेकत्वमित्यर्थः ।<br>परमाणुरूपमिति ।<br>मध्यमपरिमाणवत्त्वे<br>अनित्यत्वप्रसङ्गादित्यर्थः । ननु मनो विभु स्पर्शरहितत्वे सति द्रव्यत्वादाकाशादिवदिति<br>चेत् न । मनसो विभुत्वे आत्ममनः संयोगस्याऽसमवायिकारणस्याभावाज्ज्ञानानुत्पत्तिप्रसंङ्गः ।<br>न च विभुद्वयसंयोगोऽस्त्विति वाच्यम् । तत्संयोगस्य नित्यत्वेन सुषुप्त्यभावप्रसङ्गात्,<br>पुरीतद्व्यतिरिक्तस्थले आत्ममनः संयोगस्य सर्वदा विद्यमानत्वात् । अणुत्वे तु यदा<br>मनः पुरीतत् प्रविशति तदा सुषुप्तिः यदा निस्सरति तदा ज्ञानोत्पत्तिरित्यणुत्वसिद्धिः ।
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>Mind is defined as that which possesses activity while<br>being devoid of touch.[42. This definition of mind is free from the defect of ativyāpti in the first four dravyas beginning with Prithivī all of which are endowed with both the quality of touch and action; the last four are not endowed with either; thus, the definition does not extend to them also. Hence this definition is faultless] Mind should be taken to be different<br>in different individuals. Individuals are innumerable and,<br>correspondingly, minds also are innumerable. Mind cannot be<br>of intermediary magnitude, as in that case, it will be<br>Non-eternal.<br>[43. Manas is the last substance in the list of dravyas. As a dravya, it must have some magnitude. Now, a magnitude can be either the smallest, or the medium-sized or the largest size. The Paramāṇu is the smallest size, a perceptible substance is of the medium size, and gross elements like the sky etc are of the largest magnitude. Annambhatta asserts that mind is atomic but rejects the possibility of it being of intermediary magnitude, because, as he says, it will render Manas non-eternal inasmuch as a substance having a intermediary magnitude is a thing having parts and a thing having parts is perishable; hence, manas cannot be of medium magnitude. Similarly, manas cannot be of the ubiquitous magnitude, because, in such a magnitude there is the absence of asamavāyi kāraṇa. Now, according to the Nyāya-Vaiśeṣika doctrine, every bhāva kārya (positive product) has its asamavāyi kāraṇa. Cognition (which is a bhāva kārya) is due to the conjunction of the Ātmā with manas and this conjunction is the asamavāyi kāraṇa for an effect like cognition. Further, conjunction between two all-pervading substances is not accepted by the Nyāya-Vaiśeṣika School. And a cognition cannot rise without conjunction. Therefore, it has to be admitted that the Ātmā which is all-pervading gets involved in conjunction with manas which is not all-pervading; or else, cognition cannot take place. Thus, manas cannot be a substance having the largest magnitude. Another ground to rule out the possibility of admitting manas as having the largest magnitude is the absence of sleep if conjunction were to be admitted between two substances of largest magnitude inasmuch as such a conjunction will be eternal and, as such, consciousness too will be eternal which is contrary to experience. Hence, the Ātmā having been already admitted to be ubiquitous, the other substance, viz, manas ought to be admitted to be non-ubiquitous. Therefore, for the reasons mentioned above, manas is only of atomic size]<br>Objection: We say, mind is not atomic, but all-pervading;<br>because, it is a substance devoid of touch, like ether.<br>Reply: If mind is presumed to be all-pervading, then, there<br>will occur no cognisability on account of the absence of the<br>non-intimate cause that brings about the contact between the<br>soul and mind. Nor can it be argued, 'Let there be contact<br>between two all-pervading substances. What is wrong with<br>it?' Because, if this view-point is accepted, the occurrence<br>of absolute absence of deep sleep has to be accepted inasmuch<br>as the contact between two all-pervading substances is eternal.<br>The contact of soul and mind always exists except in a<br>small region of the nerve centre. The mind can enter the<br>nerve centre only if it is of atomic size, and cause sleep.<br>When the mind emerges out of the nerve centre, cognition<br>arises; this establishes the magnitude of the mind as atomic.
        - **Item 19**:
          - **verse_number**: `19`
          - **original_text**: SECTION III<br>DEALING WITH THE CHARACTERISTIC MARK,<br>DIVISION AND EXAMINATION OF<br>THE TWENTY FOUR QUALITIES <br>1. Colour (rūpa):<br>चक्षुर्मात्रग्राह्यो गुणो रूपम् । तच्च शुक्लनीलपीतरक्तहरितकपिशचित्र<br>भेदात्सप्तविधम् । पृथिवीजलतेजोवृत्ति । तत्र पृथिव्यां<br>सप्तविधम् । अभास्वरशुक्लं जले । भास्वरशुक्लं तेजसि ॥ १ ॥<br>[44. In the order of enumeration of the Vaiśeṣika padārthas, guna comes second. According to the Vaiśeṣika, guna as a dharma (characteristic of a thing) exists in a dravya by samavāya sambandha(inherent relation), but is, at the same time, different from Karma,<br>and višesa]
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 1. Colour is the quality apprehended only by the<br>eye. It is again of seven kinds due to its division<br>into white, blue, yellow, red, green, brown, and<br>variegated. It exists in earth, water, and light. Of these,<br>all the seven varieties exist in earth. In water, pale-white<br>variety is found; and, in light, bright-white variety resides.
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: रूपं लक्षयति । चक्षुरिति । संख्यादावतिव्याप्तिवारणाय मात्रपदम् ।<br>रूपत्वेऽतिव्याप्तिवारणाय गुणपदम् । नन्वव्याप्यवृत्तिनीलादिसमुदाय एव चित्ररूपमिति<br>चेत् न । रूपस्य व्याप्यवृत्तित्वनियमात् । ननु चित्रपटे अवयवरूपस्यैव प्रतीतिरिति<br>चेत् न । रूपरहितत्वेन पटस्याप्रत्यक्षत्वप्रसंगात् । न च रूपवत्समवेतत्वं प्रत्यक्षत्वप्रयोजकं<br>गौरवात् । तस्मात्पटस्य प्रत्यक्षत्वान्यथानुपपत्त्या चित्ररूपसिद्धिः । रूपस्याश्रयमाह ।<br>पृथिवीति । आश्रयं विभज्य दर्शयति । तत्रेति ।
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>The Text defines colour: Caksur.... The term only is<br>added to the definition to avoid the defect of overapplicability<br>in number etc.[45. The eye can apprehend number too. By inserting only in<br>the definition, the defect of ativyāpti is avoided as number is recognised<br>by touch too and not by the eye only] To avoid overapplicability in the generic<br>attribute of colour (rūpatva) the term special quality is inserted<br>in the definition. It is admitted that the same organ which<br>apprehends a quality apprehends its jātī also. The term special<br>quality is inserted to avoid overapplicability in the contact<br>of light with wall. Though the contact is a quality, it is<br>not a special quality.<br>Objection: Variegated colour is nothing but the mixture of<br>several colours like blue etc which pervade only a part of<br>objects. (So, why recognize it as a distinct colour?) [46. What the opponent intends to say is that each of the component<br>colours in a cloth of variegated colour is non-pervasive in the sense<br>that it does not characterise the whole of the thing forming its support.<br>Therefore, since component colours being non-pervasive, the compound<br>colour is nothing but a collection of these various component colours.<br>Then, where is the necessity to postulate a distinct colour like the<br>citra colour?]<br>Reply: It cannot be so; because, of the invariable law of<br>colour being a quality that resides in the entire object.[47. The above argument can be sustained only on the assumption<br>that component colours are non-pervasive entities. Annambhatta refutes<br>this by affirming that colour, as a rule, is a pervasive entity]<br>Objection: Instead of admitting of a separate single variegated<br>colour why not accept that only through the different colours<br>of its component parts a piece of cloth is apprehended as<br>having different colours?<br>Reply: If this argument is accepted, then cloth would be<br>non-perceptible inasmuch as cloth itself possesses no colour. [48. The view-point of the opponent is that what we see as<br>the citra colour is really the collection of varied colours of the<br>different parts of cloth. It is only the various coloured parts that<br>masquerade as the whole colour of the cloth. Acceptance of this<br>view-point poses a problem to Annambhatta. If the cloth as a whole<br>does not possess any colour of its own, then cloth can never become<br>an object of visual perception; for, a substance without some manifest<br>colour cannot be visually perceived. But it is perceived; then, which<br>colour is it? It cannot be the colour of any of its component parts<br>for the reason mentioned above. Therefore, the colour of the cloth<br>must be the one which is the total effect of all the colours of<br>its component parts. This total effect of all the colours is a simple<br>distinctive colour over and above the colours of its component parts.<br>And, according to Annambatta, this distinctive colour is known as<br>citra rūpa]<br>Objection: If the above argument is accepted, it will lead<br>to a conclusion that a piece of cloth is not possessing any<br>colour. A piece of cloth possesses colour but not directly.<br>There is close contact between cloth and its threads (in the<br>same sense in which a clay-made-pot is accepted to have<br>inseparable contact with clay); so, here too, cloth possesses<br>the colour on account of its being samaveta (inherent or<br>inseparably united) in its components, ie threads in which<br>colour exists.<br>Reply: Accepting such a condition for perceptibility is<br>cumbersome, and is not preferable to the usual reasoning<br>of rūpavatva (having colour). Therefore, we must recognise<br>an independent variegated colour to account for the perceptibility<br>of a variegated piece of cloth. Non-pervasiveness (avyāpya<br>vrttitvam) of a thing is the characteristic property of being<br>existent in the same substratum with its absolute negation.<br>The Text explains the substratum of colour: Prthivi....<br>The substratum is again divided by the Text: Tatra....

        - **Item 22**:
          - **verse_number**: `22`
          - **original_text**: 4. Touch (Sparśa):<br>त्वगिन्द्रियमात्रग्राह्यो गुणः स्पर्शः । स च त्रिविधः<br>शीतोष्णानुष्णाशीतभेदात् । पृथिव्यप्तेजोवायुवृत्तिः । तत्र शीतो<br>जले । उष्णस्तेजसि । अनुष्णाशीतः पृथिवीवाय्वोः ॥ ४ ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 4. Touch is the quality perceived by the tactile<br>organ alone. It is threefold: cool, hot, and lukewarm.<br>It resides in water, earth, light and air. Of these, cool<br>touch resides in water, hot touch in light and lukewarm<br>in earth and air.
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: स्पर्श लक्षयति । त्वगिति । स्पर्शत्वेऽतिव्याप्तिवारणाय गुणपदम् । संयोगादावतिव्या-<br>प्तिवाराणाय मात्रपदम् ।
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>The definition of touch is given in Text: tvagindriya. To<br>avoid overapplicability of the definition in the generic attribute<br>of touch, the word quality is inserted in the definition. Alone<br>is inserted in the definition to avoid overapplicability in<br>conjunction.[49. The insertion of alone in the definition is intended to avoid<br>the defect of ativyapti in contact of the tactile sense with a tactile<br>substance. The tactile sense is supposed to apprehend only touch,<br>either warm or cold. According to the Vaiśeşika Ontology, contact<br>also is a guna; but contact is not the same as warm or cold.<br>So, insertion of alone removes this difficulty, as contact is apprehended<br>by the visual organ also]
        - **Item 23**:
          - **verse_number**: `23`
          - **original_text**: 5. The Theory of Pāka:<br>रूपादिचतुष्टयं पृथिव्यां पाकजमनित्यं च । अन्यत्र अपाकजं<br>नित्यमनित्यं च । नित्यगतं नित्यम् अनित्यगतमनित्यम् ॥ ५ ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 5. The four qualities beginning with colour are<br>produced in earth through the application of heat, and<br>are non-eternal. In other substances (ie in water, light<br>and air) they are not produced by the application of<br>heat (ie natural) and are eternal and also non-eternal;<br>in eternals (ie in atoms) they are eternal; and in<br>non-eternals (ie in products) they are non-eternal.
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: पाकजमिति । पाकस्तेजः संयोगः । तेन पूर्वरूपं नश्यति रूपान्तरमुत्पद्यत इत्यर्थः ।<br>तत्र परमाणुष्वेव पाको न द्वयणुकादौ । आमनिक्षिप्ते घटे परमाणुषु रूपान्तरोत्पत्तौ<br>श्यामघटनाशे पुनद्वर्यणुकादिक्रमेण रक्तघटोत्पत्तिः । तत्र परमाणवः समवायिकारणम् ।<br>तेजः संयोगोऽसमवायिकारणम् । अदृष्टादिकं निमित्तकारणम् ।<br>द्वचणुकादिरूपे<br>कारणरूपमसमवायिकारणमिति पीलुपाकवादिनो वैशेषिकाः । पूर्वघटस्य नाशं विनैव<br>अवयविनि अवयवेषु च परमाणुपर्यन्तेषु युगपद्रूपान्तरोत्पत्तिरिति पिठरपाकवादिनो<br>तैयायिकाः । अत एव पार्थिवपरमाणुरूपादिकमनित्यमित्यर्थः । अन्यत्र जलादावित्यर्थः ।<br>नित्यगतमिति । परमाणुगतमित्यर्थः । अनित्यगतमिति । द्वयणुकादिगतभित्यर्थः ।<br>रूपादिचतुष्टयम् उद्भूतं प्रत्यक्षम् । अनुद्भूतमप्रत्यक्षम् । उद्भूतत्वं प्रत्यक्षत्वप्रयोजको<br>धर्मः । तदभावोऽनुद्भूतत्वम् ।
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>Contact with light is pāka. The meaning is that by the<br>contact of light with earth the colour (of the earthen product)<br>existing prior to its contact with light gets destroyed and<br>an altogether different colour is produced in its place. This<br>process of baking takes place in atoms only and not in dyads<br>etc.<br>When an unbaked jar is baked in mild heat, the jar<br>of black colour gets disintegrated (down to the stage of atoms<br>along with the qualities in those atoms); and a different colour<br>is produced (in those atoms). Again, by integration of (fresh)<br>dyads and triads, etc a new jar of red colour comes into<br>being. Here atoms form the inherent cause (of the new red<br>colour) and the contact of light forms the non-inherent cause<br>of (the new red colour of the atoms), and the unseen results<br>of the actions of the individual soul (adrsta) for whom it<br>is to be created, constitute the instrumental cause. The Vaiśeşika<br>theorists known as Pīlupākavādins hold the view that the<br>effect of colour etc is produced in atoms only and not in<br>dyads etc. But the Naiyāyikas known as Pitharapākavādins<br>hold the view that the contact of light destroys only the<br>black colour of the jar down to the stage of atoms without<br>destroying the old black jar itself and while producing<br>simultaneously an altogether different red colour in the<br>component parts down to the stage of the atoms. This is<br>why it is said that colour etc subsisting in the atoms of<br>earth are non-eternal.<br>By anyatra in the text, other substances like water, light<br>and air are meant. The word nityagatam indicates (the four<br>qualities) subsisting in the atoms. By anityagatam it is implied<br>that (the four qualities) subsisting in dyads etc are non-eternal.<br>The four qualities beginning with colour possess manifested<br>perceptibility. Those which do not possess manifest perceptibility<br>are imperceptible. Manifest perceptibility is the attribute which<br>helps in direct perception and the lack of this attribute is<br>Non-perceptibility.<br>[50. According to the Vaisesikas, the change that takes place due<br>to application of heat (pakaja change) is affected directly in the<br>ultimate (ie atomic) constituents of gross earth-substance. He gives<br>an example of an unbaked jar. When it is subjected to some degree<br>of heat, its colour changes from black to red under the influence<br>of heat. This change we see in the gross jar is because of the<br>change that undergoes directly in the atoms which are the ultimate<br>constituents of the gross object. Tejas-particles induce motion in earth<br>paramāņus; this motion effects the separation of atoms in a dvyanuka.<br>With the disintegration of dvyanuka, tryanuka also disintegrates, which<br>finally leads to the disintegration of the gross jar itself which is<br>the ultimate effect in this causal-series. Thus, it is the destruction<br>of contact of paramāņu with Tejas particles that finally results in<br>the destruction of the jar this is known as pākaja-effect induced<br>by pāka-kriyā. As the Paramāņu separates from dvyanuka, its former<br>black colour gets destroyed and in its place appears red colour under<br>the influence of contact with Tejas. These paramāņus with their changed<br>colour are the starting point of another series of causal changes<br>which finally culminates in the emergence of a red jar. Here, the<br>change is only in the quality of atoms and not in the substance<br>as such because a paramāņu is indivisible and changeless. This change<br>of quality is a positive effect (bhāva kārya) and has its samavāyi<br>kāraṇa in the paramāņu as a dravya or substance; its asamavāyi<br>kāraṇa is in its contact with Tejas particles or pāka. This contact<br>inheres in a paramāņu. The nimitta karana or the instrumental cause,<br>is in the Adrsta of the jīva for whom the jar is being produced.<br>The change of colour in a paramāņu is due to paka consisting<br>in asamavāyi kāraṇa. The red colour in a dvyanuka is due to the<br>red colour of its constituent atoms and the samavāyi kāraṇa of the<br>red colour of a dvyanuka is the dvyanuka itself and the nimitta<br>karana is the adrsta that was operative in the case of the change<br>of quality in the paramāņu.<br>While the Naiyāyikas accept the Vaisesika theory that the four<br>specific qualities of earth substance (viz rūpa, rasa, gandha and sparśa)<br>are affected by the application of heat (paka kriyā), they differ from<br>the Vaiśeşikas with regard to the process of change in colour from<br>black to red. According to the latter, pāka kriyā (ie change in colour<br>due to heat) takes place only in earth paramāņus. But the former<br>hold that like the atoms, the jar too undergoes a similar change<br>at the same time]
        - **Item 24**:
          - **verse_number**: `24`
          - **original_text**: 6. Number-Sankhyā:<br>एकत्वादिव्यवहारहेतुः संख्या । सा नवद्रव्यवृत्तिः<br>एकत्वादिपरार्धपर्यन्ता । एकत्वं नित्यमनित्यं च । नित्यगतं<br>नित्यम् । अनित्यगतमनित्यम् । द्वित्वादिकं तु<br>सर्वत्राऽनित्यमेव ॥ ६ ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 6. Number is the cause of common usage of<br>(enumerative) expressions like one, two, etc. It is found<br>in all the nine substances. It starts from one and ends<br>with parārdha. Oneness is eternal and non-eternal (ie<br>eternal in atoms and non-eternal in products); eternal<br>in eternal substances and non-eternal in non-eternals.<br>Number two and higher numbers are only non-eternals<br>everywhere.
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: संख्यां लक्षयति । एकेति ।
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>The Text defines number: Ekatvādi....<br>(Parārdha is the highest number equivalent to a lakh of<br>lakhs of crores, which constitutes half of the life-span of<br>Brahma, the creator.)
        - **Item 25**:
          - **verse_number**: `25`
          - **original_text**: 7. Dimension (Parimāṇam):<br>मानव्यवहारासाधारणकारणं<br>परिमाणम् ।<br>नवद्रव्यवृत्तिः ।<br>तच्चतुर्विधम् । अणु महत् दीर्घ ह्रस्वं चेति ॥ ७ ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 7. Dimension is the special cause of the common<br>usage of words pertaining to measurement. It is found<br>in all the nine substances. And, it is of four varieties:<br>atomic, large, long, and short.
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: परिमाणं लक्षयति । मानेति । परिमाणं विभजते । तच्चेति । भावप्रधानो निर्देशः ।<br>अणुत्वं, महत्त्वं, दीर्घत्वं, ह्रस्वत्वं चेत्यर्थः ।
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>The Text defines dimension: Māna.... Dimension is divided:<br>taccha.... Aṇu, etc in the Text indicate aṇutva etc that is<br>to say, minuteness, largeness, length and shortness.<br>[51. Further, parimāņa is a real feature of a thing and is not<br>something just fancied about; eg when we say, this thing is big,<br>we really intend to describe the thing with certain specification with<br>reference to its size which is not a mere subjective whim but an<br>objective reality. As Realists, the Vaisesikas term this feature of a<br>substance as parimāna or dimension. To exclude Iśvara, time, dik<br>and adrsta which are the common causes of all products, the word<br>'special' is inserted in the definition. The dimension of atoms known<br>as pārimandalya - infinitesimality and the dimension of vibhu-dravyas<br>(all-pervasive substances), are eternal; all the rest are non-eternal]
        - **Item 26**:
          - **verse_number**: `26`
          - **original_text**: 8. Distinctness (Pṛthaktvam):<br>पृथग्व्यवहारासाधारणकारणं पृथक्त्वम् । सर्वद्रव्यवृत्तिः ॥ ८ ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 8. Distinctness is the special cause of conventional<br>expressions such as this is distinct from that. It is found<br>in all the nine substances.
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: पृथक्त्वं लक्षयति । पृथगिति । इदमस्मात्पृथक् इति व्यवहारकारणं पृथक्त्वमित्यर्थः ।
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>The Text defines distinctness: prthak.... That which causes<br>the expression of distinctness of one object from another<br>is severalty. <br>[52. It has been argued that distinctness can as well be considered<br>a kind of abhāva (absence) known as anyonyābhāva (ie mutual absence).<br>Vaiśeşikas do not accept this theory as it would go against their<br>enumeration of qualities as twenty four; further, the apprehension<br>this is distinct from that is a positive experience; it being so, to<br>consider it a mere absence is doing violence to our consciousness;<br>hence, it is preferable to accept this awareness as a quality rather<br>than as a kind of absence]
        - **Item 27**:
          - **verse_number**: `27`
          - **original_text**: 9. Conjunction (Samyoga):<br>संयुक्तव्यवहारहेतुः संयोगः । सर्वद्रव्यवृत्तिः ॥ ९ ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 9. Conjunction is the special cause of conventional<br>expressions of contact between two objects. It exists<br>in all the substances.
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: संयोगं लक्षयति । संयुक्तेति । इमौ संयुक्तौ इति व्यवहारहेतुरित्यर्थः । संख्यादिलक्षणे<br>सर्वत्र दिक्कालादावतिव्याप्तिवारणाय असाधारणेति विशेषणीयम् । संयोगो द्विविधः कर्मजः<br>संयोगजश्वेति । आद्यो हस्तक्रियया हस्तपुस्तकसंयोगः । द्वितीयो<br>हस्तपुस्तकसंयोगात्कायपुस्तकसंयोगः । अव्याप्यवृत्तिः संयोगः । स्वात्यन्ताभावसमाना-<br>धिकरणत्वमव्याप्यवृत्तित्वम् ।
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>The Text defines conjunction: samyukta.... The expression<br>which causes the idea: these are in conjunction with each other,<br>is conjunction. Everywhere the term special ought to be given<br>while denoting the characteristic mark of number etc in order<br>to remove the defect of overapplicability in space, time, etc.<br>Conjunction is of two varieties: born of action and of contact.<br>The former arises when a book comes in contact with hand,<br>due to the activity of the hand in grasping a book. The<br>second variety is the contact which arises between one's body<br>taken as a whole and a book, when the book is in contact<br>with one's hand. Conjunction is a non-pervasive (quality).<br>Non-pervasiveness in a thing is the characteristic quality of<br>being co-existent in the same substratum with its absolute<br>negation. <br>[53. According to the Vaiśeşikas, conjunction (samyoga) is a quality<br>inhering in a substance. It is an objective reality due to which the<br>expression signifying conjunction is possible, eg when the book is<br>on the table. It is again of two kinds, born of action or direct,<br>and born of contact itself or indirect; eg the contact of a hand<br>and a book is born of action and is the direct samyoga. Indirect<br>samyoga is due to samyoga itself, that is to say, the direct contact<br>of a book with the hand indirectly results in the contact of the<br>book with the entire body of which the hand is only a part.<br>Further, according to the Vaiśeşikas, samyoga is a avyāpya vṛtti,<br>ie non-pervasive property. This non-pervasiveness is when the thing<br>and its absence have the same substratum; eg when the hand is<br>in contact with the table, the absence of that contact also is in<br>the table (taking the entire table as the substratum), or, it also might<br>be that even when the hand is in contact with the table, there<br>is the awareness that the hand is not a part of the table. Thus,<br>avyāpya comes to be defined as being co-existent in the same locus<br>where there is also the absolute negation]
        - **Item 28**:
          - **verse_number**: `28`
          - **original_text**: 10. Disjunction (Vibhāga):<br>संयोगनाशको गुणो विभागः । सर्वद्रव्यवृत्तिः ॥ १० ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 10. Disjunction is the quality which destroys<br>conjunction. It resides in all the nine substances.
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: विभागं लक्षयति । संयोगेति । कालादावतिव्याप्तिवारणाय गुण इति ।<br>रूपादावतिव्याप्तिवारणाय संयोगनाशक इति । विभागोऽपि द्विविधः कर्मजो विभागजश्वेति ।<br>आद्यो हस्तपुस्तकविभागः । द्वितीयो हस्तपुस्तकविभागात्कायपुस्तकविभागः ।
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>The Text defines disjunction: samyoga.... The word quality<br>is inserted in the definition to avoid over-applicability in time<br>etc. To avoid overapplicability in colour etc the term destroyer<br>of contact is used in the definition. Disjunction is also of<br>two kinds: born of action and born of disjunction. The first<br>variety is the disjunction of a book from the hand caused<br>by the action of the hand (in throwing away the book).<br>The second variety is the disjunction of the book from the<br>body caused by the disjunction of the book from the hand.
        - **Item 29**:
          - **verse_number**: `29`
          - **original_text**: 11. Remoteness - Paratva and Proximity (aparatva):<br>परापरव्यवहारासाधारणकारणे परत्वापरत्वे । पृथिव्यादिचतुष्टय<br>मनोवृत्तिनी । ते द्विविधे दिक्कृते कालकृते च । दूरस्थे दिक्कृतं<br>परत्वं । समीपस्थे दिक्कृतमपरत्वम् । ज्येष्ठे कालकृतं परत्वम् ।<br>कनिष्ठे कालकृतमपरत्वम् ॥ ११ ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 11. Remoteness and proximity are the special causes<br>of common usage of words expressing remoteness and<br>proximity. They reside in four substances beginning with<br>earth, and also in mind. Each of them is of two varieties:<br>spatial and temporal. In things existing at a distance,<br>spatial remoteness is found; in things near, spatial<br>proximity is found; in an elderly person, temporal<br>remoteness is found; in a younger person, temporal<br>proximity is found.
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: परव्यवहारासाधारणकारणं परत्वम् ।<br>परत्वापरत्वयोर्लक्षणमाह । परापरेति ।<br>अपरव्यवहारासाधारणकारणमपरत्वम् इत्यर्थः । ते विभजते । ते द्विविधे इति ।<br>दिक्कृतयोरुदाहरणमाह । दूरस्थइति । कालकृते उदाहरति । ज्येष्ठ इति ।
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>The Text defines remoteness and proximity: Para.... The<br>special cause of expression of posteriority is remoteness. The<br>special cause of expression of priority is proximity. Remoteness<br>and proximity are divided by the Text: te dvividhe.... The<br>example for remoteness and proximity caused by spatial distance<br>and nearness is shown: dūrasthe.... The example for remoteness<br>and proximity caused by temporal distance and nearness is<br>shown in the Text: jyesthe....
        - **Item 30**:
          - **verse_number**: `30`
          - **original_text**: 12. Heaviness (Gurutvam):<br>आद्यपतनासमवायिकारणं गुरुत्वम् । पृथिवीजलवृत्ति ॥ १२ ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 12. Heaviness is the non-intimate cause of the first<br>down-ward motion in a falling substance. It resides<br>in earth and water.
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: गुरुत्वं लक्षयति । आद्येति । द्वितीयादिपतनस्य वेगासमवायिकारणत्वाद्वेगेऽतिव्याप्तिवारणाय<br>आद्येति ।
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>The Text defines Heaviness: ādya.... Velocity is the<br>non-intimate cause of the second and subsequent acts of<br>falling of a substance. Therefore, to avoid overapplicability<br>(of the definition) in velocity, the word ādya is used in<br>the definition.
        - **Item 31**:
          - **verse_number**: `31`
          - **original_text**: 13. Fluidity - (dravatvam):<br>आद्यस्यन्दनासमवायिकारणं द्रवत्वं । पृथिव्यप्तेजोवृत्ति ।<br>त‌द्विविधं, सांसिद्धिकं नैमित्तिकं च । सांसिद्धिकं जले ।<br>नैमित्तिकं पृथिवीतेजसोः । पृथिव्यां घृतादावग्नि संयोगजं<br>द्रवत्वम् । तेजसि सुवर्णादौ ॥ १३ ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 13. Fluidity is the non-intimate cause of the first<br>flow (of a liquid substance). It resides in earth, water<br>and light. It is of two varieties: natural and artificial.<br>In water, natural fluidity is found. In earth and light,<br>artificial fluidity is found. In earth artificial fluidity is<br>generated through the contact of fire with ghee. Artificial<br>fluidity is found in gold etc belonging to light.
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: द्रवत्वं लक्षयति । आद्येति । स्यन्दनं प्रस्रवणम् । तेजःसंयोगजं नैमित्तिकम् । तद्भिन्नं<br>सांसिद्धिकम् । पृथिव्यां नैमित्तिकमुदाहरति । घृतादाविति । तेजसि तदाह ।<br>सुवर्णादाविति ।
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>The Text defines fluidity: ādyasyandana.... Flow is the<br>act of flowing of a liquid substance. The fluidity generated<br>through the contact of light is artificial fluidity. The one other<br>than this is the natural fluidity. An example of artificial fluidity<br>in earth is given in ghee coming in contact with fire. In<br>the same way, artificial fluidity in light is exemplified in gold.<br>(The word adya is inserted in the definition to exclude velocity<br>which is the non-intimate cause of the second and all subsequent<br>acts of flowing).
        - **Item 32**:
          - **verse_number**: `32`
          - **original_text**: 14. Viscidity (Snehah):<br>चूर्णादिपिण्डीभावहेतुर्गुणः स्नेहः । जलमात्रवृत्तिः ॥ १४ ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 14. Viscidity is the quality which causes the<br>agglutination of powder etc. It exists in water only.
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: स्नेहं लक्षयति । चूर्णेति । कालादावतिव्याप्तिवारणाय गुणपदम् । रूपादावतिव्याप्तिवारणाय<br>पिण्डीभावेति ।
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>The Text defines viscidity: curna.... The word quality<br>is inserted in the definition to exclude time etc (which are<br>the common causes of all effects). To avoid the defect of<br>over-applicability in colour etc the word Pindībhāva etc is<br>used in the definition.<br>[54. Sneha is the special quality which helps solidification by<br>bringing about a peculiar combination among particles of powder.<br>To the argument that fluidity is the agglutinating agent and not sneha,<br>Naiyāyikas answer that if it is so, liquefied gold should be the cause<br>of agglutination of powder etc. But liquefied gold does not seem<br>to possess this quality of agglutination. So, the agent that causes<br>agglutination is sneha]
        - **Item 33**:
          - **verse_number**: `33`
          - **original_text**: 15. Sound (Sabdah):<br>श्रोत्रग्राह्यो गुणः शब्दः, आकाशमात्रवृत्तिः । स द्विविधः,<br>ध्वन्यात्मकः वर्णात्मकश्च । तत्र ध्वन्यात्मकः भेर्यादौ । वर्णात्मकः<br>संस्कृतभाषादिरूपः ॥ १५ ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 15. Sound is the quality apprehended by the organ<br>of hearing. It subsists in ether alone. It is twofold:<br>of the nature of inarticulate sound and of the nature<br>of the alphabetic sound. Inarticulate sound is found<br>in the sound produced by drums etc. The alphabetic<br>sound is found in the form of languages like Sanskrit<br>etc.
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: शब्दं लक्षयति । श्रोत्रेति । शब्दत्वेऽतिव्याप्तिवारणाय गुणपदम् । रूपादावतिव्याप्तिवारणाय<br>श्रोत्रेति । शब्दास्त्रिविधः संयोगजः, विभागजः, शब्दजश्वेति । तत्र आद्यो<br>भेरीदण्डसंयोगजन्यः । द्वितीयो वंशे पाटचमाने दळद्वयविभागजन्यश्चटचटाशब्दः ।<br>भेर्यादिदेशमारभ्य श्रोत्रदेशपर्यन्तं द्वितीयादिशब्दाः शब्दजाः ।
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>The Text defines sound: śrotra.... The word quality is<br>inserted in the definition to avoid the defect of overapplicability<br>in soundness.[55. Since soundness too is perceived by the organ of hearing.<br>Soundness being the generic attribute of sound (jāti), gets excluded<br>by the insertion of quality in the definition] To avoid the defect of overapplicability in<br>colour etc the word śrotra is used in the definition. Sound<br>is of three kinds: born of conjunction; born of disjunction;<br>and born of sound. The first kind of sound is produced<br>by the contact of a stick with a drum; the decrepitating<br>sound that is produced when a bamboo is halved is the<br>second kind of sound; all subsequent sounds reaching the<br>ear from the place where the sound is produced first, is<br>the third kind of sound.<br>[56. Sound is three-fold according to its source of origination.<br>The peculiar samyoga between two material bodies (like a stick and<br>a drum) produces one type of sound. The second variety of sound<br>is produced due to vibhāga disjunction, eg when a bamboo is<br>split. The third variety is when a series of sound is produced by<br>the initial sound. When a drum is hit by a stick, it produces the<br>first sound by means of samyoga. This first one generates the second<br>sound which in turn generates the third sound and so on and on<br>till the sound reaches the far-off auditory organs. This is the third variety of sound]
        - **Item 34**:
          - **verse_number**: `34`
          - **original_text**: 16. Cognition (Jñānam):<br>सर्वव्यवहारहेतुर्गुणो बुद्धिर्ज्ञानम् । सा द्विविधा<br>स्मृतिरनुभवश्च ॥ १६ ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 16. Cognition is the quality which is the cause<br>of all kinds of communications; and it is knowledge.<br>It is two-fold: remembrance and apprehension.
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: बुद्धेर्लक्षणमाह । सर्वव्यवहारेति । कालादावतिव्याप्तिवारणाय गुण इति ।<br>रूपादावतिव्याप्तिवारणाय सर्वव्यवहार इति । जानामीत्यनुव्यवसायगम्यं ज्ञानमेव लक्षणमिति<br>भावः । बुद्धिं विभजते । सेति ।
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>The characteristic mark of cognition is given in the Text:<br>sarva.... The word quality is inserted in the definition to<br>avoid overapplicability in time etc. To avoid overapplicability<br>in colour etc the term sarvavyavahāra... is inserted in the<br>definition. The characteristic mark of cognition is: the immediate<br>knowledge which becomes the subject (gamya) of the<br>consciousness (anuvyavasāya) having the form: I know. <br>[57. The word vyavahāra in the definition means apt usage of appropriate words. Vyavasaya jñāna is the simple knowledge of a thing, like, this is a jar. In Anuvyavasaya jñāna, the cognition this is a jar becomes the subject matter of the consciousness I know, ie it is visaya jñāna-visayakam jñānam. According to the Nyaya-Vaiśeşka doctrine, anuvyavasaya jñāna is subsequent to vyavasaya jñāna. According to them, the first cognitive state is not self-luminous, ie it does not become the subject of an internal perception. Perceiving a table is a state of cognition; at that moment there is no precise cognition I see a table. It is only at a subsequent instant that he has the consciousness that he sees a table. This subsequent subjective perception is anuvyavasaya jñāna]
        - **Item 35**:
          - **verse_number**: `35`
          - **original_text**: 17. Cognition is twofold: (i) Remembrance (Smrti) and<br>(ii) Apprehension: (anubhava):<br>(i) Remembrance (Smrti):<br>संस्कारमात्रजन्यं ज्ञानं स्मृतिः ॥ १७ ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 17. Remembrance is the knowledge born of mental<br>impressions alone.
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: स्मृतेर्लक्षणमाह । संस्कारेति । भावनाख्यः संस्कारः । संस्कारध्वंसेऽतिव्याप्तिवारणाय<br>ज्ञानमिति । घटादिप्रत्यक्षेऽतिव्याप्तिवारणाय संस्कारजन्यमिति ।<br>अतिव्याप्तिवारणाय मात्रपदम् ।
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>प्रत्यभिज्ञायाम्<br>Remembrance is defined by the Text: samskāra. Mental<br>impression is reminiscent impression. [58. Mental impression is threefold: vega, bhāvanā, and sthitisthāpaka. Of these, only the bhāvanā variety is to be taken here] The word jñānam is<br>inserted in the definition to avoid overapplicability in the<br>destruction of mental impression (samskāradhvamsa).[59. Samskaradhvamsa is also born of samskāra, as without samskāra there can be no samskāra-dhvarmsa. Therefore, there is ativyāpti in the definition samskāramātra janyam. To avoid this, jñānam is added to the definition] The term<br>born of samskāra is added (to the definition) to avoid<br>overapplicability in perception of things like jar etc. To avoid<br>overapplicability in reminiscence (pratyabhijñā), the term alone<br>is used in the definition. <br>[60. Identification of the cognition of a thing or a person with its or his recollection of past impression is pratyabhijñā reminiscence. Recognition thus results when immediate cognition arising from the contact of organ with the object of perception is identical with the recollection of past impressions. For instannce, when we say: verily, this is that man, this refers to the cognition arising from the immediate perception of man consequent on the contact of organ; and that refers to the recollection of past impressions relating to the same man. Thus, by inserting the term alone, the defect of overapplicability in reminiscence is avoided. Remembrance (smrti) is born of mental impression alone whereas pratyabhijñā is born out of perception also]
        - **Item 36**:
          - **verse_number**: `36`
          - **original_text**: 18. Apprehension (anubhava):<br>तद्भिन्नं ज्ञानमनुभवः । स द्विविधः, यथार्थोऽयथार्थश्च ॥ १८ ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 18. Knowledge other than recollection is<br>apprehension. It is twofold: conformable to the object<br>and not conformable to the object.
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: अनुभवं लक्षयति । तद्भिन्नमिति । स्मृतिभिन्नं ज्ञानमनुभव इत्यर्थः । अनुभवं विभजते ।<br>स द्विविध इति ।
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>Apprehension is defined by the Text: Tadbhinnam. The<br>meaning is that knowledge other than recollection is<br>apprehension. Apprehension is divided by the Text: sa dvividhah.
        - **Item 37**:
          - **verse_number**: `37`
          - **original_text**: 19. Valid apprehension (Yathārtha anubhava):<br>तद्वति तत्प्रकारकोऽनुभवो यथार्थः । यथा रजते इदं रजतमिति<br>ज्ञानम् । सैव प्रमेत्युच्यते ॥ १९ ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 19. A valid apprehension is that in which an object<br>is known as possessing attributes which it really possesses<br>eg the apprehension of silver arising in an object where<br>there is silverness. This is also known as valid knowledge<br>of a thing.
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: यथार्थानुभवस्य लक्षणमाह । तद्वतीति । ननु घटे घटत्वम् इति प्रमायामव्याप्तिः,<br>घटत्वे घटाभावादिति चेत् न, यत्र यत्संबन्धोऽस्ति तत्र तत्संबन्धानुभवः इत्यर्थाद्धटत्वे<br>घटसंबन्धोऽस्तीति नाव्याप्तिः । सैवेति । यथार्थानुभव एव शास्त्रे प्रमेत्युच्यत इत्यर्थः ।
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>The characteristic mark of valid apprehension is explained<br>by the Text: Tadvati....<br>Objection: If the above definition is admitted, it results<br>in partial applicability in true apprehension: potness in a pot;<br>because, jar does not exist in its genus potness. [61. The idea is this: The above definition of pramā applies to a cognition of jar when we interpret tadvati to mean ghatavati; but this cannot apply to a cognition of ghatatva itself as existing in a ghata, because, ghatatva is not the adhikarana of ghata or any other thing. The expression ghate ghatatvam amounts to saying: ghata vrtti ghatatvam, potness residing in a pot. Vrttitvam or ādheyatā is in potness. Through the link, viz ādheyatā (the quality of residing), pot can be accepted to reside as prakāra in potness if adheyatā were accepted as a sambandha of pot and potness. But ādheyatā as such is not recognised as a sambandha (relation) at all. So, pot cannot reside in potness as a prakāra. Accordingly. ghatavati (ghatatve) ghata prakāraka jñānam does not happen. As such partial applicability in the true apprehension potness in a pot has to be accepted]<br>Reply: It is not so; because where a certain relation exists,<br>there also occurs the apprehension of that relation. [62. When tadvati is interpreted to mean tatsambandhavati, pot<br>becomes the substratum of ghatatvasarmbandha, because ghatasambandha,<br>exists in ghatatva; SO ghatatva becomes the adhikarana of<br>ghatasambandha. Thus, tatsambandhavati (ghatasambandhavati ghatatve)<br>tatprakāraka jñānam (ghataprakāraka jñānam) happens] Therefore<br>there is no defect of avyāpti in the above definition. This<br>valid cognition is also known as pramā in the scriptures.
        - **Item 38**:
          - **verse_number**: `38`
          - **original_text**: 20. Erroneous apprehension (ayathārtha anubhava):<br>तदभाववति तत्प्रकारकोऽनुभवोऽयथार्थः । यथा<br>शुक्ताविदंरजतमिति ज्ञानम् । सैव अप्रमा इत्युच्यते ॥ २० ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 20. That is non-valid apprehension in which an<br>object is known as having an attribute which it does<br>not have in reality. For example, the apprehension of<br>silver arising in a piece of mother-of-pearl. This indeed<br>is called the false cognition.
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: अयथार्थानुभवं लक्षयति । तदभाववतीति । नन्विदं संयोगीति प्रमायामतिव्याप्तिरिति<br>चेत् न । यदवच्छेदेन यत्संबन्धाभावस्तदवच्छेदेन तत्संबन्धज्ञानस्य विवक्षितत्वात् ।<br>संयोगाभावावच्छेदेन संयोगज्ञानस्य भ्रमत्वात्संयोगावच्छेदेन संयोगसंबन्धस्य<br>सत्त्वान्नातिव्याप्तिः ।
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>The Text defines erroneous cognition: tadabhāvavati... [63. Apramā anubhava is when a cognition has for its prakāra<br>its inherent jāti while, in reality, the thing suffers from the absence<br>of that very prakāra; eg the cognition of a snake. Snake must have<br>the characteristic of snakeness in it in order to render that cognition<br>valid. Suppose, what really exists before one is a rope and not<br>a snake; the snakeness which is the prakāra in the above cognition<br>is absent in a rope which has for its feature only ropeness. Hence,<br>the cognition of snake is faulty inasmuch as there is no snakeness<br>in it; hence it is apramā anubhava]<br>Objection: The above definition brings in overapplicability<br>in the apprehension: this is in conjunction.[64. The idea of the objection is this: conjunction possesses the<br>characteristic of partial pervasiveness. Therefore, the adhikarana has<br>both conjunction and also the negation of conjunction. For example,<br>when one says, the monkey is sitting on a tree, one gets the knowledge<br>that the tree is in conjunction with monkey. But the definition of<br>apramā also applies to this apprehension; because, here we have<br>kapi samyogaprakāraka anubhava in an adhikarana where there is<br>also the absence of kapisamyoga in the lower part of the tree]<br>Reply: No; it is only in erroneous apprehension that the<br>knowledge of contact is obtained from a part where there<br>is the absence of contact and what is implied here by apramā<br>is the knowledge of contact in a particular space where there<br>is the absence of contact. But in the above apprehension<br>(kapisamyogīti jñāne), contact is known to exist in the space<br>where there is the contact of monkey. So there is no defect<br>of overapplicability.
        - **Item 39**:
          - **verse_number**: `39`
          - **original_text**: 21. Valid apprehension is fourfold:<br>यथार्थानुभवः चतुर्विधः प्रत्यक्षानुमित्युपमितिशाब्दभेदात् ॥ २१ ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase

            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>Valid apprehension is divided by the Text: Yathārtha....
        - **Item 40**:
          - **verse_number**: `40`
          - **original_text**: 22. The instruments of valid apprehension:<br>तत् करणमपि चतुर्विधं प्रत्यक्षानुमानोपमानशब्द भेदात् ॥ २२ ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 22. Their instruments too are fourfold due to division<br>into the instrument of perception, instrument of inference,<br>instrument of comparative knowledge; and the instrument<br>of verbal testimony.
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: प्रसङ्गात्प्रमाकरणं विभजते । तत्करणमपीति । प्रमाकरणमित्यर्थः । प्रमाकरणं प्रमाणमिति प्रमाणसामान्यलक्षणम् ।
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>In this context, the means of valid apprehension is also being explained.[65. Having mentioned valid apprehension, there arises the need<br>to mention the means of valid apprehension. So, the means are<br>being classified and explained by the Text] That which gives rise to right apprehension is proof; this definition is the general characteristic of proof.
        - **Item 41**:
          - **verse_number**: `41`
          - **original_text**: 23. Definition of Special cause:<br>असाधारणं कारणं करणम् ॥ २३ ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 23. The uncommon cause (instrumental or efficient) is the special cause.
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: करणलक्षणमाह । असाधारणेति । दिक्कालादावतिव्याप्तिवारणाय असाधारणेति ।
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>The text defines cause: asādhārana.... The term uncommon is inserted in the definition to avoid the defect of overapplicability in time, space etc. <br>[66. There are two types of causes: common cause - sādhārana<br>kāraņa, and uncommon or special cause - asādhāraņa kārana. That<br>which is the cause of every effect is the general or common cause,<br>eg space, time, destiny etc. That which is the specific cause of<br>an effect is called the special or uncommon cause, eg yarn in the<br>production of cloth]
        - **Item 42**:
          - **verse_number**: `42`
          - **original_text**: 24. Definition of cause:<br>कार्यनियतपूर्ववृत्ति कारणम् ॥ २४ ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 24. That which invariably precedes its effect is its cause.
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: कारणलक्षणमाह । कार्येति । पूर्ववृत्ति कारणमित्युक्ते रासभादावतिव्याप्तिः स्यादतो नियतेति । तावन्मात्रे कृते कार्येऽतिव्याप्तिरतः पूर्ववृत्तीति । ननु तन्तुरूपमपि पटं प्रति कारणं स्यादिति चेत् न, अनन्यथासिद्धत्वे सतीति विशेषणात् । अनन्यथासिद्धत्वमन्यथासिद्धिरहितत्वं । अन्यथासिद्धिः त्रिविधा - येन सहैव यस्य<br>यं प्रति पूर्ववृत्तित्वमवगम्यते तं प्रति तेन तदन्यथासिद्धम् । यथा तन्तुनां तन्तुरूपं<br>तन्तुत्वं च पटं प्रति । अन्यं प्रति पूर्ववृत्तित्वे ज्ञात एव यस्य यं प्रति<br>पूर्ववृत्तित्वमवगम्यते तं प्रति तदन्यथासिद्धम् । यथा शब्दं प्रति पूर्ववृत्तित्वे ज्ञात<br>एव पटं प्रत्याकाशस्य । अन्यत्र क्लृप्तनियतपूर्ववतिर्न एव कार्यसंभवे तत्सहभूतमन्यथासिद्धम् ।<br>यथा पाकजस्थले गन्धं प्रति रूपप्रागभावस्य । एवञ्च अनन्यथासिद्धनियतपूर्ववृत्तित्वं<br>कारणत्वम् ।
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>The text defines the cause; kārya... If cause is defined<br>merely as antecedent, the defect of overapplicability occurs<br>in animals like ass etc (in the production of a jar). This<br>is avoided by the inclusion of the term invariably (as an<br>ass need not be invariably antecedent in the production of<br>a jar). Again, the mere definition kāryaniyatam kāraņam, brings<br>in the defect of overapplicability in the produced effect. [67. That is, when so defined, it means that wherever there is<br>product, there is product. Thus ativyapti occurs in kārya as niyatatva<br>is found in effect also.]<br>To avoid this, the term pūrvavṛtti is inserted in the definition<br>(ie there can be no antecedent effect before the production<br>of an effect).<br>Objection: The colour of the yarn also becomes the cause<br>of cloth (as both kāryaniyatatva and antecedence are found<br>in colour).<br>Reply: It is not so; because the definition of karana is<br>qualified by the adjunct ananyathāsiddhatve sati which means<br>provided, the antecedent thing is not connected with dispensable<br>things. Ananyathāsiddha is that which does not depend for<br>its invariable existence upon some other antecedent existing<br>thing. [68. The idea is this: The absence of dispensable antecedent is<br>anan yatāsiddha. There will not happen the absence of dispensable<br>antecedents when there is the presence of indispensable cause; for<br>example, the existence of ether and kapālarūpa etc in the production<br>of a jar, are the dispensable antecedents. In these anyathāsiddhas,<br>both invariability and antecedence are found, thus causing the defect<br>of ativyāpti in the definition of karana. To avoid this, the term<br>ananyathāsiddha is added to the definition] The absence of dispensable antecedents is<br>ananyathäisiddha.<br>Anyathāsiddhatva (the dispensable antecedence in the<br>production of a thing) is threefold: (a) Things that are connected<br>to inherent relation (samavāya sambandha) with the cause<br>and thus become the antecedents to effect through them;<br>as for instance, the colour of yarn (tanturūpa) and the generic<br>attribute of yarn (tantutva) which are intimately united with<br>yarn, are antecedent to its effect, viz, a piece of cloth (pata).<br>Here both tanturūpa and tantutva are the examples for the<br>first variety of anyathāsiddha. (b) Things that are antecedent<br>to cause and therefore become antecedent to effect; as for<br>instance, ether in the production of a jar. Ether is not directly<br>perceived and its existence is proved only with reference<br>to sound of which ether is the inherent cause. Thus, ether<br>becomes antecedent to jar only after it becomes antecedent<br>to its effect, sound. Therefore, ether is anyathāsiddha in the<br>production of a jar. This is an example for the second type.<br>(c) All other concomitants of cause that are not connected<br>with it by inherence (samavāya). Since the production of an<br>effect is determined by its invariable antecedence in some<br>other place, other antecedents that accompany the product<br>are considered anyathāsiddha: for instance, when heat is applied,<br>the antecedent non-existence of colour (rūpa prāgabhāva) is<br>anyathāsiddha in the production of smell. This is the third<br>type. Thus a cause is defined as the indispensable invariable<br>antecedence of an effect. <br>[69. A mango, possessing all the qualities except smell, gets the<br>quality of smell when subjected to heat. Here the causes of the<br>production of odour in the fruit must be the antecedent non-existence<br>of smell inasmuch as there cannot be any other antecedent non-existence<br>except the antecedent non-existence of smell, in view of the maxim<br>of the Naiyāyikas that without the existence of antecedent non-existence<br>no effect can be produced; otherwise, we should be able to produce<br>another jar from the clay of an already existing jar. Another mango<br>possessing all the four qualities except colour and smell, is subjected<br>to warming. In the process, the fruit gets both colour and smell.<br>Here a doubt arises as to which antecedent non-existence is the<br>cause in the production of smell. In places other than the application<br>of heat, the antecedent non-existence of smell is determined to be<br>the cause in the production of smell. Therefore, here also, the antecedent<br>non-existence of smell is essential in the production of smell. Therefore,<br>the antecedent non-existence of colour is anyathāsiddha here. This<br>is the example for the third variety of anyathāsiddhatva]
        - **Item 43**:
          - **verse_number**: `43`
          - **original_text**: 25. Definition of effect:<br>कार्यं प्रागभावप्रतियोगि ॥ २५ ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 25. Effect is the counter-correlative of its own<br>antecedent non-existence (in its inherent cause).
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: कार्यलक्षणमाह । कार्यमिति ।
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>The Text gives the characteristic mark of effect: kāryam....<br>[70. Annambhatta defines effect - kārya as the Pratiyogin or<br>counter correlative of its own antecedent non-existence. Pratiyogin is<br>used in the context of either abhāva (absence or non-existence) or<br>sambandha (relation). In the former, pratiyogin refers to the object<br>whose absence is intended to be established. For ghalābhāva, the<br>ghata is the pratiyogin of its abhāva. For the abhava of ghatābhāva,<br>ghatābhāva is the pratiyogin. Effects like a pot, a table, etc come<br>into being at a certain point of time which proves its antecedent<br>non-existence as otherwise expressions such as 'will come into existence'<br>would be redundant. So, before the effect comes into existence,<br>there was its absence; this is known as antecedent non-existence<br>or prāgabhāva and an effect like a ghata is the pratiyogin of such<br>antecedent non-existence]
        - **Item 44**:
          - **verse_number**: `44`
          - **original_text**: 26. Cause (Kāranam):<br>कारणं त्रिविधम् - समवाय्यसमवायिनिमित्तभेदात् ॥ २६ ॥<br>27. The inherent cause (samavāyi kāraṇam):<br>यत् समवेतं कार्यं उत्पद्यते तत् समवायिकारणम् ।<br>यथा तंतवः पटस्य, पटश्च स्वगतरूपादेः ॥ २७ ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 26. Cause is of three kinds: inherent, non-inherent,<br>and occasioning (instrumental or efficient).<br>[71. Nyāya-Vaiśeşika School recognises that every positive effect<br>has three causes, viz, samavāya, asamavāya, and nimitta; eg in a<br>product like cloth, yarn is the inherent cause; the contact of two<br>yarns is the non-inherent cause; and causes other than these two,<br>like the weaver, shuttle etc are the efficient causes. Inherent cause<br>is always a substance; non-inherent cause must be either a quality<br>or an action and nothing else; while nimitta kāraņa may be of any<br>kind. Abhāva has only nimitta kāraņa]<br>27. The inherent cause is that which is in inseparable<br>union in (the thing in) which the effect is produced,<br>as the yarns are of the (effect) cloth and the cloth<br>is of the colour on it.
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: कारणं विभजते । कारणमिति । समवायिकारणस्य लक्षणमाह — यत्समवेतमिति ।<br>यस्मिन् समवेतमित्यर्थः । असमवायिकारणं लक्षयति — कार्येणेति । कार्येणेत्येतदुदाहरति<br>— तन्तुसंयोग इति ।
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>The Text defines inherent cause: Yat samavetam.... The<br>idea is that it is in inseparable union with the effect. <br>[72. Samavāyi kāraņa is defined as that inhering in which an<br>effect emerges, eg the yarn which constitutes the intimate cause of<br>its effect cloth, because the cloth is connected with the yarns by<br>inherent union. Similarly, the colour on the piece of cloth being<br>a quality, resides in the cloth by intimate relation. Therefore, the<br>piece of cloth is the inherent cause of the colour on it]
        - **Item 45**:
          - **verse_number**: `45`
          - **original_text**: 28. The non-inherent cause (asamavāyi kāraṇam):<br>कार्येण कारणेन वा सह एकस्मिन्नर्थे समवेतत्वे सति यत्कारणं<br>तदसमवायिकारणम् । यथा तन्तुसंयोगः पटस्य, तन्तुरूपं<br>पटरूपस्य ॥ २८ ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 28. The non-inherent cause is one which is<br>inseparably united in the same object either with the<br>effect, or with the cause, as the conjunction of yarns<br>in relation to the (effect) cloth or the colour of the<br>yarns in relation to (the effect) the colour of the cloth.<br>[73. Here taritusamyoga inheres in yarn with cloth which is the<br>effect of the contact of yarns. Thus, both the cloth and the tantusamyoga<br>exist in the same substratum, ie tantus. Therefore, tantusamyoga is<br>the non-inherent cause to cloth]
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: कार्येण पटेन सह एकस्मिंस्तन्तौ समवेतत्वात्तन्तुसंयोगः पटस्यासमवायिकारणमित्यर्थः ।<br>कारणेनेत्येतदुदाहरति - तन्तुरूपमिति । कारणेन पटेन सह एकस्मिंस्तंन्तौ समवेतत्वात्
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>The Text gives two definitions of non-intimate cause:<br>the first is: kāryeņa saha ekasminnarthe samavetatve sati yat<br>kāraņam tat asamavāyikāraņam that is the non-inherent cause,<br>which is inherent in the same substratum along with the<br>effect. The Text provides the example of the relation of contact<br>of yarn with cloth to explain this type of non-inherent cause.<br>The second variety is: kāraṇena saha ekasminnarthe<br>samavetatve sati yat kāraņam tat asamavāyikāraņam that is<br>the non-inherent cause which is inherent in the substratum<br>along with cloth which is the inherent cause of the colour<br>of cloth (patarūpa).<br>[74. The example cited here is the relationship of tanturūpa to<br>patarūpa. Here the colour of yarns inheres in yarns along with cloth<br>which is the inherent cause of patarūpa, that is to say, the colour<br>of yarns produces the colour of cloth in cloth inhering in yarn along<br>with cloth. Therefore, the colour of yarn is the non-inherent cause<br>to the colour of cloth]
        - **Item 46**:
          - **verse_number**: `46`
          - **original_text**: 29. Occasioning cause (nimitta kāraṇam):<br>तदुभयभिन्नं कारणं निमित्तकारणम् ।<br>यथा तुरीवेमादिकं पटस्य ॥ २९ ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 29. The cause which is different from both these<br>causes is the occasioning cause (instrumental or efficient),<br>such as the shuttle, loom etc in the case of (production<br>of) a piece of cloth.
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: तन्तुरूपं पटरूपस्यासमवायिकारणमित्यर्थः । निमित्तकारणं लक्षयति तदुभयेति ।<br>समवाय्यसमवायिभिन्नकारणं निमित्तकारणमित्यर्थः ।
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>The Text defines the occasioning cause: tadubhaya.... The<br>cause different from both the intimate and non-intimate causes<br>is the occasioning cause (either instrumental or efficient).<br>[75. A cause which is other than either the samavāyi or the<br>asamavāyi kāraṇa, is the nimitta kāraṇa, eg the activities of the<br>weaver, the shuttle, the loom etc]
        - **Item 47**:
          - **verse_number**: `47`
          - **original_text**: 30. Special Cause (asādharanam kārannam):<br>तदेत‌त्त्रिविधकारणमध्ये यदसाधारणं कारणं तदेव<br>करणम् ॥ ३० ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 30. That alone which is the uncommon cause among<br>the three varieties of causes mentioned above, is the<br>special cause.
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: करणलक्षणमुपसंहरति तदेतदिति ।
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>The Text concludes the definition of special cause:<br>Tadetat.... <br>[76. Kāraṇa is defined by Annambhatta as an uncommon or special<br>cause. Keśavamiśra in his Tarkabhāṣā defines karaṇam as sādhakatamam,<br>ie the most immediate cause in bringing about an effect, that is<br>to say, it is the most efficient cause. That which directly and immediately<br>brings about the result by its own action is karaṇam, eg an arrow<br>in killing a bird. Here, arrow is the special cause which brings about<br>the instant death of a bird, because, it alone immediately brings<br>about the result killing the bird by its own activity of striking the<br>bird dead. Thus karaṇa is a cause which is most essential in producing<br>a result and without the activity of which the effect is not produced<br>in spite of the other causes being present. This is the view held<br>by the modern Naiyāyikas. But the ancient Naiyāyikas define it as<br>vyāpāravat asādhāraṇam kāraṇam karaṇam ie karaṇa is a special and<br>operative cause. Vyāpāra is an operation which, not being a substance,<br>is the product of a thing, and produces the effect of that thing.<br>The example for this is as follows: The sense organ is karaṇa.<br>The contact of a sense-organ with its corresponding object is the<br>vyāpāra. The percept is the effect. The contact of sense-organs happens<br>to be both cause and effect. It becomes cause with reference to<br>the effect of organ and becomes also effect with reference to sense<br>organ. That which is known as vyāpāra by the ancient Naiyāyikas<br>is known as karaṇa by the modern Naiyāyikas]
        - **Item 48**:
          - **verse_number**: `48`
          - **original_text**: SECTION IV<br>THE NYAYA-VAISESIKA<br>THEORY OF EPISTEMOLOGY<br>31. Perception (Pratyaksa Pramāņa):<br>तत्र प्रत्यक्षज्ञानकरणं प्रत्यक्षम् ॥ ३१ ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 31. Among them, perception is the special cause<br>of percept.
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: प्रत्यक्षलक्षणमाह<br>तत्रेति । प्रमाणचतुष्टयमध्ये इत्यर्थः ।
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>The Text defines percept: tatra.... Tatra means, among<br>the four means of apprehension.
        - **Item 49**:
          - **verse_number**: `49`
          - **original_text**: 32. Perception defined and explained:<br>इन्द्रियार्थसन्निकर्षजन्यं ज्ञानं प्रत्यक्षम् । त‌द्विविधं निर्विकल्पकं<br>सविकल्पकं चेति । तत्र निष्प्रकारकं ज्ञानं निर्विकल्पकं यथेदं<br>किश्चित् । सप्रकारकं ज्ञानं सविकल्पकं यथा डित्थोऽयं,<br>ब्राह्मणोऽयं, श्यामोऽयं, पाचकोऽयमिति ॥ ३२ ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 32. Perception is the apprehension born of the<br>contact of the organ with an object. It is of two kinds:<br>indeterminate and determinate. Of these, the knowledge<br>of a thing without any attribute is known as the<br>indeterminate apprehension; such as, this is something.<br>Attributive knowledge is determinative apprehension.<br>Apprehensions like, he is a Dittha, he is a Brahmaņa,<br>he is black, he is a cook, are examples of determinate<br>apprehension.
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: प्रत्यक्षज्ञानस्य लक्षणमाह — इन्द्रियेति । इन्द्रियं चक्षुरादिकम्, अर्थो घटादिः,<br>तयोः संनिकर्षः संयोगादिः, तज्जन्यं ज्ञानमित्यर्थः । तद्विभजते । तद्विविधमिति ।<br>निर्विकल्पकस्य लक्षणमाह — निष्प्रकारकमिति । विशेषणविशेष्यसंबन्धानवगाहि<br>ज्ञानमित्यर्थः । ननु निर्विकल्पके किं प्रमाणमिति चेत् न, गौरिति विशिष्टज्ञानं<br>विशेषणज्ञानजन्यं, विशिष्टज्ञानत्वात्, दण्डीति ज्ञानवत् इत्यनुमानस्य प्रमाणत्वात् ।<br>विशेषणज्ञानस्यापि सविकल्पकत्वेऽनवस्थाप्रसङ्गान्निर्विकल्पकसिद्धिः । सविकल्पकं<br>लक्षयति । सप्रकारकमिति । नामजात्यादिविशेष्यविशेषणसम्बन्धावगाहि ज्ञानमित्यर्थः ।<br>सविकल्पकमुदाहरति । यथेति ।
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>The distinguishing characteristic mark of perception is given<br>in the Text: Indriya.... The organs are the eye etc. Objects<br>are jar etc. Their connection is known as contact. That which<br>is produced as a result of this contact (of organ with the<br>object) is perception. It is again divided by the Text: it is<br>of two kinds. The Text then gives the characteristic mark<br>of indeterminate apprehension. The knowledge in which there<br>is the absence (anavagahi) of a subject, attribute, and relation<br>(between the attribute and the subject) is the indeterminate<br>knowledge.<br>Question: What is the proof to determine the indeterminate<br>knowledge?<br>Reply: The attributive knowledge: this is a cow, is born<br>of the knowledge of the qualifying attribute cowness; because,<br>it is an attributive knowledge (viśistajñāna) like the knowledge,<br>A man with a stick. Inference is thus the proof (in establishing<br>the indeterminate knowledge). If we say that the knowledge<br>of the attribute also is determinate knowledge, it will then<br>and us in the logical anomaly of infinitum regressus. Therefore,<br>the knowledge of the attribute proved by inference, has to<br>be accepted to determine the indeterminate knowledge. The<br>knowledge which comprehends the relation of the qualified<br>and the qualifier such as name, class etc is known as the<br>determinate knowledge. Determinate knowledge is exemplified:<br>yathā.... The Text defines determinate knowledge:<br>Saprakārakam....<br>[77. The four examples given in the text are in illustration of<br>samjñā, name; jāti class; guṇa quality; and kriyā - action]
        - **Item 50**:
          - **verse_number**: `50`
          - **original_text**: 33. The operative cause for perception is six-fold:<br>प्रत्यक्षज्ञानहेतुरिन्द्रियार्थसंनिकर्षः षड्विधः । संयोगः, संयुक्तसम-<br>वायः, संयुक्तसमवेतसमवायः, समवायः, समवेतसमवायः,<br>विशेषणविशेष्यभावश्चेति ॥ ३३ ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 33. The conjunction of organs with objects which<br>causes perception is of six kinds: (a) conjunction; (b)<br>inherent union with what has come into contact; (c)<br>inherent union with what is intimately united with a<br>thing which has come in contact; (d) inherent union;<br>(e) inherent union with the intimately united; and (f)<br>the connection of the attribute with the substantive.
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: इन्द्रियार्थसंनिकर्ष विभजते । प्रत्यक्षेति ।

        - **Item 51**:
          - **verse_number**: `51`
          - **original_text**: 34. The first operative cause (Samyoga - sannikarsah):<br>चक्षुषा घटप्रत्यक्षजनने संयोगः सन्निकर्षः ॥ ३४ ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 34. Conjunction is the contact, producing perception<br>of the jar by the eye.
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: संयोगसंनिकर्षमुदाहरति । चक्षुषेति । द्रव्यप्रत्यक्षे सर्वत्र संयोगः संनिकर्षः । आत्मा<br>मनसा संयुज्यते, मन इन्द्रियेण, इन्द्रियमर्थन, ततः प्रत्यक्षज्ञानमुत्पद्यते इत्यर्थः ।
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>The Text exemplifies conjunctive contact: cakṣuṣā....<br>Conjunctive contact is the cause in producing perception of<br>substances in all cases. The self (ātman) comes in contact<br>with mind (manas), the mind with the organ (indriya), and<br>organ with the object (visaya), and then perceptual knowledge<br>takes place. <br>[78. According to the Nyāya doctrine, direct perception is always<br>dependent on some relation between an Indriya (sense organ) and<br>an artha (object). This relation is the operative cause in bringing<br>about a cognition. The cognition may either be laukika (ordinary)<br>perception of bhāva padārthas (positive entities) like that of a dravya,<br>guṇa, karma or jāti. Again, we may have perception of a quality<br>like śabda in ākāśa which is not perceived by the senses; there<br>occurs also the perception of the universal soundness abiding in sound.<br>We may have also perception of negative entities like, the absence<br>of a pot or a table etc. In each case what is necessary is a certain<br>kind of relation between the artha (object) perceived and the respective<br>indriya (sense organ) involved in the act of perception. This perception<br>is again twofold: Savikalpaka (determinant) and Nirvikalpaka<br>(indeterminant) which has been dealt with earlier]
        - **Item 52**:
          - **verse_number**: `52`

            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: समवायमुदाहरति । श्रोत्रेणेति । तदुपपादयति । कर्णेति । ननु दूरस्थशब्दस्य कथं<br>श्रोत्रसम्बन्ध इति चेत् न, वीचीतरङ्गन्यायेन कदम्बमुकुलन्यायेन वा शब्दान्तरोत्पत्तिक्रमेण<br>श्रोत्रदेशे जातस्य श्रोत्रेण संबन्धात्प्रत्यक्षसंभवः ।
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>The Text gives an example of inherent union: śrotrena.<br>That is being elaborated Karna....<br>Question: How does a distant sound come in contact with<br>the auditory sense?<br>Reply: Just like the principle of vīcītaranga or<br>kadambamukula; the first sound produces another sound which<br>in turn propels another, and so on, till the last sound reaches<br>the auditory sense, thus enabling one to perceive sound directly. <br>[81. Vīcītaranga nyāya is the principle of a wave urging forward<br>another wave. In the ocean one wave propels another, till the first<br>and all the subsequent waves reach the shore. In the same way,<br>there is a successive operation in the case of the production of<br>sound till they reach the auditory orifice. Kadambamukula nyāya indicates<br>the simultaneous blossoming forth of the buds in a kadamba tree<br>when a cloud thunders in the sky]
