        - **Item 56**:
          - **verse_number**: `56`
          - **original_text**: 39. Sixth operative cause (Viśeṣana<br>Sannikarsa):<br>Viśeşyabhāva<br>अभावप्रत्यक्षे विशेषणविशेष्यभावः सन्निकर्षः, घटाभाव-<br>वद्भूतलमित्यत्र चक्षुः संयुक्ते भूतले घटाभावस्य विशेषणत्वात् ॥ ३९ ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 39. The connection of the qualifier and the qualified<br>is the conjunction in the perception of negation, as<br>in the cognition: This spot of earth is with the negation<br>of jar. Here, the negation of a jar is an attribute<br>of a place in contact with the eye.
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: विशेषणविशेष्यभावमुदाहरति । अभावेति । तदुपपादयति । घटाभाववदिति । भूतले<br>घटो नास्तीत्यत्र अभावस्य विशेष्यत्वं द्रष्टव्यम् । एतेन अनुपलब्धेः प्रमाणान्तरत्वं<br>निरस्तम् । यद्यत्र घटोऽभविष्यत्तर्हि भूतलमिवाद्रक्ष्यत, दर्शनाभावान्नास्तीति<br>तर्कितप्रतियोगिसत्त्वविरोध्यनुपलब्धिसहकृतेन्द्रियेणैव अभावज्ञानोत्पत्तौ अनुपलब्धेः<br>प्रमाणान्तरत्वासंभवात् । अधिकरणज्ञानार्थमपेक्षणीयेन्द्रियस्यैव करणत्वोपपत्तावनुपलब्धेः<br>करणत्वायोगात् । विशेषणविशेष्यभावो विशेषणविशेष्यस्वरूपमेव, नातिरिक्तः संबन्धः ।<br>प्रत्यक्षज्ञानमुपसंहरंस्तस्य करणमाह । एवमिति ।
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>The Text gives an example of the connection of the<br>attribute and the subject: abhāvah.... This is being explained:<br>Ghatābhāvavat.... In the cognition this spot of earth is with<br>the negation of jar, the spot of earth is the qualified and the<br>negation of jar is the attribute. [82. In this cognition, the eye is in contact with the spot of<br>earth of which the negation of jar is the attribute, and, therefore,<br>here, the connection is, contact with the attribute] In the cognition, there is<br>no jar on the spot of earth, (where the eye is in contact<br>with the spot of earth of which) the negation of jar is the<br>qualified. [83. Hence, here, the connection is, contact with the qualified.<br>Both these cognitions though differing in form, are identical in meaning.<br>They are conjointly expressed in the Text as viśeṣaṇaviśeşya bhāva.<br>With the contact of the eye with the spot of earth in the above<br>two cognitions, the negation either on account of its possessing the<br>property of Viśeşya or of Viśeṣaṇa becomes perceived on the spot<br>of earth. The relation between the spot of earth and the negation<br>of pot is no other than Visesana-Viśeşya bhāva] By this, the necessity for accepting non-perception<br>as a distinct source of knowledge (anupalabdheḥ pramānāntarat-<br>vam) is refuted.<br>If there had been a jar, then the jar would have been<br>apprehended as existing on that spot of earth. It is due to<br>its absence, the apprehension, jar is not here, arises. Here,<br>in this argument, is found the counter-entity of the negation<br>of apprehension, thus proving that non-perception is only an<br>accessory (sahakarn) of the eye which alone perceives the<br>negation of jar on earth. Therefore (in this knowledge of<br>the negation of jar on earth), the eye alone being the cause<br>of the knowledge of substratum of negation it is impossible<br>to state that anupalabdhi (non-perception) is an independent<br>source to gain the knowledge of negation of jar.[84. The Nyaya School of thought does not accept the necessity<br>of postulating a distinct source of valid knowledge (pramāṇa) to cognise<br>absence (abhāva) as this could as well be achieved by perception<br>in which the operative causal relation is between the viśeṣaṇa and<br>viśeşya. The absence is apprehended on the same locus (adhikaraṇa).<br>And this perception of abhāva of a certain thing on a certain locus,<br>is not to be taken to be merely due to the defect of the eye<br>or due to insufficient light; but it ought to be preceded by Tarka,<br>that is to say, by the thought like the following: if a pot were on<br>the ground, I should be seeing it right now, but I do not see the<br>pot. This Tarka brings in the awareness of the absence of the pot<br>on the ground. It is in order to emphasize such effective perception<br>of absence that Annambhatta mentions in his Dīpikā about the existence<br>of the pratiyogi (counter entity) of the negation of apprehension.<br>This further proves that non-perception is necessary for the immediate<br>apprehension of abhāva; here, non-apprehension is an auxiliary condition<br>(sahakari) of the eye which alone perceives the ghatābhāva on a<br>spot of earth. Therefore, admission of a separate pramāņa like<br>anupalabdhi is redundant when the same can be had by pratyaksa<br>— this is the Naiyāyika stand-point]<br>The relationship between the qualifier and the qualified<br>is nothing but the nature of the attribute and the qualified.<br>By this no other relationship is meant.
        - **Item 57**:
          - **verse_number**: `57`
          - **original_text**: 40. Conclusion:<br>एवं सन्निकर्षषट्कजन्यं ज्ञानं प्रत्यक्षं, तत्करणमिन्द्रियम्,<br>तस्मादिन्द्रियं प्रत्यक्षप्रमाणमिति सिद्धम् ॥ ४० ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 40. Perception is thus the cognition caused by the<br>sixfold contact, and the organ is its cause (efficient<br>or instrumental). Therefore, organ is the proof of<br>perception.
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: असाधारणकारणत्वादिन्द्रियं प्रत्यक्षज्ञानकरणमित्यर्थः ।प्रत्यक्षप्रमाणमुपसंहरति ।<br>तस्मादिति ।

Since the organ<br>is the special cause of perception, it is called the efficient<br>or instrumental cause of perception. He concludes the section<br>dealing with pratyakṣa pramāṇa by the words: tasmāt....
        - **Item 58**:
          - **verse_number**: `58`
          - **original_text**: SECTION V<br>INFERENCE<br>1. Inference (anumānam):<br>अनुमितिकरणमनुमानम् ॥ १ ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 1. Inference is the special cause of inferential<br>knowledge.
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: अनुमानं लक्षयति-अनुमितिकरणमिति ।
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>The Text defines inference: anumitikaranam.<br>[85. Inference is treated after direct perception inasmuch as the<br>former depends on the latter]
        - **Item 59**:
          - **verse_number**: `59`
          - **original_text**: 2. Inferential knowledge (anumiti):<br>परामर्शजन्यं ज्ञानमनुमितिः ॥ २ ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 2. Inferential knowledge is the knowledge born of<br>subsumptive reflection.
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: अनुमितिं लक्षयति -- परामर्शेति । ननु संशयोत्तरप्रत्यक्षेऽतिव्याप्तिः स्थाणुपुरुषसंशयानन्तरं<br>पुरुषत्वव्याप्यकरादिमानयम् इति परामर्शे सति पुरुष एव इति प्रत्यक्षजननात् ।<br>न च तत्रानुमितिरेवेति वाच्यम् । पुरुषं साक्षात्करोमि इत्यनुव्यवसायविरोधादिति<br>चेत् न, पक्षतासहकृतपरामर्शजन्यत्वस्य विवक्षितत्वात् । सिषाधयिषाविरहविशिष्टसिद्धयभावः<br>पक्षता । साध्यसिद्धिरनुमितिप्रतिबन्धिका । सिद्धिसत्त्वेऽपि अनुमिनुयाम्<br>इतीच्छायामनुमितिदर्शनात् सिषाधयिषोत्तेजिका । ततश्चोत्तेजकाभावविशिष्टमण्यभावस्य<br>दाहकारणत्ववत्सिषाधयिषाविरहविशिष्टसिद्धयभावस्याप्यनुमितिकारणत्वम् ।
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>The Text gives the characteristic mark of inferential<br>knowledge: parāmarśa....<br>Objection: There is overapplicability in perception in the<br>form of a conclusion arrived at after first doubting, as in<br>the example: Is this a post or a man? (On the perception<br>of an object from a distance in insufficient light, the doubt,<br>'is this a post or a man?' arises). Then he reasons with<br>the help of subsumptive reflection: But this object has hands<br>etc which are found invariably associated with mankind. Then<br>arises the perception: this indeed is a man. It should not<br>be argued that the above cognition this indeed is a man<br>is to be construed as inferential knowledge inasmuch as it<br>has been derived consequent on parāmarśa, viz, this has hands<br>etc which is vyāpya (less pervasive) with regard to mankind,<br>and as it is contrary to anuvyavasaya, like: I am perceiving<br>a man. <br>[86. By raising the above objection the opponent means to say<br>that in samsayottara pratyaksa a kind of intermediary parāmarśa operates<br>inasmuch as perceptual experience arises following consideration of<br>certain particulars of the object, thus causing the defect of ativyāpti<br>in samsayottara pratyaksa. This defect in the definition is avoided<br>by prefixing parāmarsa with the adjunct, pakṣatāsahakṛta]<br>Reply: Not so; because, the defect in the definition is<br>avoided by prefixing parāmarśa with the adjunct: in association<br>with pakṣatā. Then it ought to be interpreted as follows:<br>inferential cognition is the knowledge that arises from parāmarśa<br>in association with paksatā. Paksatā (ie the characteristic of<br>an instance) consists in having in it the negation of the<br>ascertainment of the thing to be inferred, accompanied by a<br>negation of any desire to infer an already proven thing [87. In samśayottara pratyaksa, there is no paksatājanyatva, because,<br>man and his hands etc are perceived simultaneously, and as such,<br>no sādhya is left to be ascertained, and also there could be no<br>desire to infer. Thus the defect of ativyāpti is removed. Again, if<br>it is merely said, parāmarśājanyam anumitih, the defect of ativyāpti<br>occurs in parāmarsadhvamsa, as it is also parāmarśajanya. To avoid<br>this, the term jñānam is inserted in the definition.] Indubious<br>knowledge of the thing to be proved is the counter-acting factor<br>in inferential knowledge (ie when the thing to be proved is<br>ascertained in the paksa, the need to infer its existence is<br>superfluous). Even when the thing to be proved is perceived,<br>sometimes it is observed that a desire: I shall infer, stimulates<br>one to take to inference (to establish the proven). Here,<br>the factor that stimulates one (to take to inference) is the<br>desire to establish a thing in the paksa. Just as the absence<br>of a pratibandhaka mani (the gem capable of neutralising<br>the burning power of fire) in association with the absence<br>of a uttejaka mani (a gem that stimulates the power of burning<br>in fire) is the cause of heat, even so, the absence of indubious<br>knowledge of the thing to be proved in association with the<br>absence of a desire to predicate a thing in the paksa is<br>the cause of inferential knowledge. <br>[88. When a particular variety of gem capable of neutralising the<br>burning power of fire is placed near fire, the gem obstructs the<br>burning power of fire; but, if some stimulator gem is placed near<br>the counter-acting agent, it effectively neutralises the latter. Here,<br>the absence of a desire to establish a thing — sişādhayişāvirahatva<br>is given as a delimiting adjunct — viśeşana to indubious knowledge<br>— siddhi, and not to the absence of indubious knowledge. The absence<br>of stimulating-gem is given as a delimiting adjunct to the obstructor-gem.<br>When the obstructor-gem is present in association with the<br>stimulator-gem, there will be the power of burning in fire even like<br>power of burning remaining intact when there is the absence of<br>obstructor-gem in association with the absence of stimulator-gem.<br>Similarly, of the two, viz, (1) indubious knowledge, and, (2) a desire<br>to predicate a thing in the paksa, the former is the obstructing<br>agent — pratibandhaka, while the latter is the stimulator agent — uttejaka.<br>Inferential knowledge is brought about consequent on the absence<br>of indubious knowledge in association with the absence of a desire<br>to establish or predicate a thing]
        - **Item 60**:
          - **verse_number**: `60`
          - **original_text**: 3. Subsumptive reflection (parāmarśa):<br>व्याप्तिविशिष्टपक्षधर्मताज्ञानं परामर्शः । यथा वह्निव्याप्यधूमवानयं<br>पर्वत इति ज्ञानं परामर्शः । तज्जन्यं पर्वतो वह्निमानिति<br>ज्ञानमनुमितिः ॥ ३ ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 3. Subsumptive reflection is the knowledge of reason<br>existing in the subject accompanied by invariable<br>concomitance (of sādhya), eg the knowledge: this<br>mountain has smoke which is invariably pervaded by fire<br>is parāmarsa; and the cognition that results from it<br>and takes the form: the mountain is fiery is the inferential<br>knowledge.
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: परामर्श लक्षयति व्याप्तीति । व्याप्तिविषयकं यत्पक्षधर्मताज्ञानं स परामर्श<br>इत्यर्थः । परामर्शमभिनीय दर्शयति यथेति । अनुमितिमभिनीय दर्शयति<br>तज्जन्यमिति । परामर्शजन्यमित्यर्थः । व्याप्तिलक्षणमाह यत्रेति ।
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>The Author defines parāmarśa: vyāpti.... The meaning is<br>that the knowledge of the existence of reason in an instance<br>which has invariable concomitance as its object, is subsumptive<br>reflection. The Author explains parāmarśa by declaration through<br>the words: Yathā.... Similarly, he explains inferential knowledge<br>through the usage of words, tajjanyam.... The meaning is:<br>inferential knowledge is born of subsumptive reflection.
        - **Item 61**:
          - **verse_number**: `61`
          - **original_text**: 4. Invariable concomitance - vyāpti:<br>यत्र यत्र धूमस्तत्र तत्राग्निरिति साहचर्यनियमो व्याप्तिः ॥ ४ ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 4. Invariable concomitance is the certainty of<br>co-existence like, wherever there is smoke there is fire.
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: यत्र धूमस्तत्राग्निः इति व्याप्तेरभिनयः । साहचर्यनियमः इति लक्षणम् । साहचर्य<br>सामानाधिकरण्यं, तस्य नियमः । हेतुसमानाधिकरणात्यन्ताभावाप्रतियोगिसाध्यसामाना-<br>धिकरण्यं व्याप्तिरित्यर्थः ।
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>The characteristic mark of vyāpti is given in the Text:<br>yatra.... The Text further illustrates vyāpti through the words:<br>where there is smoke there is also fire. The distinguishing<br>characteristic mark of invariable concomitance is the invariability<br>of concomitance. Concomitance is co-existence (of smoke and<br>fire) in one and the same substratum; its certainty is niyama.<br>The fuller definition of vyāpti is: the quality of being co-existent<br>in the same substratum with the thing to be proved which<br>does not become the counter-entity of its own absolute negation<br>that co-exists with reason in the same substratum. <br>[89. Take for example, smoke and fire. The said vyapti becomes<br>applicable to smoke. What could be the negation that co-exists in<br>the mountain with the reason, smoke? It can be the negation of<br>pot, but not the negation of fire, because where there is smoke,<br>there is also fire. Fire thus cannot become the counter-entity of<br>its negation. With that fire (which does not become the counter-entity<br>to its own negation that co-exists with reason, smoke) there is<br>co-existence to smoke. So, the said vyāpti is in the smoke)]
        - **Item 62**:
          - **verse_number**: `62`
          - **original_text**: 5. Paksadharmata - The special feature of a subject:<br>व्याप्यस्य पर्वतादिवृत्तित्वं पक्षधर्मता ॥ ५ ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 5. The presence of an invariably concomitant thing<br>in an object like a mountain makes it the characteristic<br>of a receptacle or subject. <br>[90. When fire is intended to be established on a hill because<br>of the presence of smoke, it is a valid inference. Here, fire is<br>the sādhya, (probandum) hill is the paksa (or subject) and smoke<br>is the hetu or sadhana (probans). The perception of smoke on the<br>hill leads to the inference of existence of fire on the hill, that is<br>to say, on the perception of hetu on the paksa, the sādhya is<br>inferred. But, this perception of hetu (smoke) on the paksa (hill)<br>leads to correct inference of the existence of sādhya (fire) only if<br>the hetu in question is invariably associated with sādhya everywhere.<br>So, it is not the mere presence of smoke by itself that leads to<br>inference; it must be smoke as qualified by the invariable association<br>with fire. This relation is vyapti and the smoke is the vyāpya, ie<br>that which is characterised by vyapti. So, pakşadharmata (the special<br>feature of the paksa hill) is defined as the presence of vyāpya<br>(smoke) in the paksa (hill)]
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: पक्षधर्मतास्वरूपमाह - व्याप्यस्येति ।
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dipika:

        - **Item 64**:
          - **verse_number**: `64`
          - **original_text**: 7. (a) Inference for oneself:<br>तत्र स्वार्थं स्वानुमितिहेतुः, तथाहि, स्वयमेव भूयोदर्शनेन यत्र<br>यत्र धूमस्तत्र तत्राग्निरिति महानसादौ व्याप्तिं गृहीत्वा पर्वतसमीपं<br>गतः, तद्गते चाग्नौ सन्दिहानः पर्वते धूमं पश्यन् व्याप्तिं<br>स्मरति यत्र यत्र धूमस्तत्र तत्राग्निः इति । तदनन्तरं<br>वह्निव्याप्यधूमवानयं पर्वत इति ज्ञानमुत्पद्यते, अयमेव लिङ्गपरामर्श<br>इत्युच्यते । तस्मात् पर्वतो वह्निमानिति ज्ञानमनुमितिः उत्पद्यते ।<br>तदेतत् स्वार्थानुमानम् ॥ ७ ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 7. Of them, inference for oneself is the cause of<br>one's own inferential knowledge. For instance, one, having<br>repeatedly seen in the hearth etc the invariable<br>concomitance of smoke with fire, arrives at the universal<br>generalisation: wherever there is smoke there is fire, and<br>goes near a mountain. On suspecting fire on the mountain,<br>and seeing smoke thereon, he remembers the universal<br>concomitance: wherever there is smoke there is fire. After<br>this, there dawns in him the knowledge this mountain<br>is smoky, pervaded by fire. This is known as subsumptive<br>reflection arising from a mark. From the above knowledge,<br>the inferential knowledge, mountain has fire in it arises.<br>This is one's own inference.
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: अनुमानं विभजते अनुमानमिति । स्वार्थानुमानं दर्शयति स्वयमेवेति । ननु<br>पार्थिवत्वलोहलेख्यत्वादौ शतशः सहचारदर्शनेऽपि वज्रमणौ व्यभिचारोपलब्धेर्भूयोदरनिन<br>कथं व्याप्तिग्रह इति चेत् न । व्यभिचारज्ञानविरहसहकृतसहचारज्ञानस्य व्याप्तिग्राहकत्वात् ।<br>व्यभिचारज्ञानं निश्चयः शङ्का च । तद्विरहः कचित्तर्कात्, कचित्स्वतः सिद्ध एव ।<br>धूमाग्न्योर्व्याप्तिग्रहे कार्यकारणभावभङ्गप्रसङ्गलक्षणस्तर्को व्यभिचारशङ्कानिवर्तकः । ननु<br>सकलवह्निधूमयोरसन्निकर्षात्कथं व्याप्तिग्रह इति चेत् न । वह्नित्वधूमत्वरूप-<br>सामान्यप्रत्यासत्त्यासकलवह्निधूमज्ञानसंभवात् । तस्मादिति । लिङ्गपरामर्शादित्यर्थः ।
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>Tatra in the Text means between the two kinds of inference.<br>Bhuyodarśanena in the Text means acceptance of the invariable<br>concomitance of smoke with fire after repeated ascertainment<br>of invariable co-existence of the thing to be proved and the<br>reason.<br>Objection: One may observe a hundred times the co-existence<br>of earth and the possibility of its being scratched by metals;<br>still, in things like diamond, a deviation to the above rule<br>is observed (ie though vajra is a particular modification of<br>earth, no scratches can be caused by any metal except vajra).<br>Then, how can it be said that invariable concomitance is<br>ascertained by repeated observation?<br>[91. The opponent is questioning the sustainability of the statement:<br>one having repeatedly seen in the hearth etc the invariable concomitance<br>of smoke with fire, arrives at the certain generalization: wherever<br>there is smoke there is fire. This statement is wrong inasmuch as<br>there exists a deviation in things like vajra. In the inference, wherever<br>there is pārthivatva there is lohalekhyatva', pārthivatva is vyāpya and<br>lohalekhyatva is vyāpaka. This vyāpti jñāna is gained by repeated<br>perception of their co-existence. But this vyāptijñāna, gained by repeated<br>observation, is defective inasmuch as it is vitiated from vyabhicāra<br>dosa, ie presence of hetu in a place where there is the absence<br>of sādhya. Here, in vajra, pārthivatva, is present but lohalekhyatva<br>is absent, ie hetu is present where there is the absence of sadhya.<br>Therefore, bhūyodarśana cannot be accepted as the right means to<br>establish vyāpti]<br>Reply: The objection is not tenable, because, vyāpti is to<br>be interpreted as the knowledge of co-existence in association<br>with an absence of fallacious knowledge. Fallacious knowledge<br>is twofold; certainty of a contrary fact, and mere suspicion.<br>The absence of vyabhicarajñāna is proved sometimes by<br>reasoning; sometimes it is self-proved from lack of reason.<br>Vyabhicāra śaikā in the statement of the invariable concomitance<br>of smoke and fire is removed by the argument of the possibility<br>of breaking the relation of effect and cause.<br>[92. The meaning is this: the suspicion, why not there be smoke,<br>though no fire exists? is untenable since it breaks the law of relation<br>of cause and effect between fire and smoke. Smoke is the effect<br>of fire. So, there is kāryakāraṇa bhāva between smoke and fire.<br>Therefore, entertainment of vyabhicāra śańkā in a context like this<br>is untenable]<br>Objection: How can this invariable concomitance of smoke<br>and fire be accepted when there is the absence of perception<br>of all fires and all smokes existing everywhere?<br>Reply: One can have the knowledge of all types of fires<br>and smokes existing in all the places by means of perception<br>of the generic attributes of fire and smoke. Tasmāt in the<br>text means from linga parāmarsa, ie from the subsumptive<br>reflection arising from a mark.
        - **Item 65**:
          - **verse_number**: `65`
          - **original_text**: 8. b) Inference for others:<br>यत्तु स्वयं धूमादग्निमनुमाय परंप्रतिबोधयितुं पञ्चावयव वाक्यं<br>प्रयुज्यते तत् परार्थानुमानम् । यथा पर्वतो वह्निमान्, धूमत्वात्,<br>यो यो धूमवान् स वह्निमान् यथा महानसः, तथा चायं,<br>तस्मात्तथेति । अनेन प्रतिपादितात् लिङ्गात् परोऽप्यग्निं<br>प्रतिपद्यते ॥ ८ ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 8. But when, one, having inferred for oneself fire<br>from smoke, employs the five-membered syllogism to<br>make others understand the same, it is known as<br>'inference for others', eg the mountain is fiery, because<br>of smoke; whichever is smoky is fiery, as a hearth, and<br>this is such, and therefore it is so. From this five membered<br>syllogism, others also apprehend fire (on the mountain<br>by means of inference).
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: परार्थानुमानमाह - यत्त्विति । यच्छब्दस्य 'तत्परार्थानुमानम्' इति तच्छब्देनान्वयः ।<br>पञ्चावयववाक्यमुदाहरति - यथेति ।
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>The Text explains 'inference for another': yat tu.... The<br>word yat has to be taken in agreement with the word tat<br>as by the word yat (that) inference for another is meant.<br>The five membered syllogism is exemplified: yathā....
        - **Item 66**:
          - **verse_number**: `66`
          - **original_text**: 9. The five-membered syllogism<br>प्रतिज्ञा - हेतु - उदाहरण - उपनय - निगमनानि पञ्चावयवाः ।<br>पर्वतो वह्निमानिति प्रतिज्ञा । धूमवत्वात् इति हेतुः ।<br>यो यो धूमवान् स वह्निमान् यथा महानस इत्युदाहरणम् ।<br>तथा च अयमिति उपनयः । तस्मात्तथेति निगमनम् ॥ ९ ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 9. The five members of the syllogism are: the<br>proposition set down; the reason adduced therein; the<br>example; the subsumptive correlative; and the conclusion.<br>The proposition set down is, the mountain is fiery,<br>the reason adduced in support of the above proposition<br>is, due to smokiness; the example cited is, whichever<br>is smoky is fiery, as a hearth; the subsumptive correlative<br>is, and so this mountain (has smoke pervaded by fire);<br>therefore it is such is the conclusion.
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: अवयवस्वरूपमाह प्रतिज्ञेति ॥ उदाहृतवाक्ये प्रतिज्ञादिविभागमाह पर्वतो<br>वह्निमानिति ॥ साध्यवत्तया पक्षवचनं प्रतिज्ञा । पञ्चम्यन्तं लिङ्गप्रतिपादकं हेतुः ।<br>व्याप्तिप्रतिपादकं उदाहरणम् । व्याप्तिविशिष्टलिङ्गप्रतिपादकं वचनमुपनयः । हेतुसाध्यवत्तया<br>पक्षप्रतिपादकं वचनं निगमनम् । अबाधितत्वादिकं निगमनप्रयोजनम् ।
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>The Text expalins the nature of the limbs: pratijñā....<br>Through sentences of examples the Text explains the sections<br>like pratijña etc: parvato vahnimān.... The declaration of the<br>subject (receptable paksa in which the sādhya is to be<br>ascertained) along with the thing to be proved is proposition.<br>The declaration indicating the mark that serves to demonstrate<br>the sādhya in the paksa and which (ie the declaration) ends<br>in the ablative case, is the reason. The declaration which<br>ascertains the invariable concomitance of reason and the thing<br>to be proved, is the example. The declaration of mark<br>accompanied by the invariable concomitance of the thing to<br>be proved is the subsumptive correlative. The declaration of<br>the subject along with the thing to be proved and reason,<br>is the conclusion. The knowledge of the thing to be ascertained<br>in a subject is the purpose of proposition. The utility of<br>reason is the indication of the knowledge of the mark in<br>a subject. The purpose of exemplification is to demonstrate<br>the invariable concomitance (between the hetu and the sādhya).<br>The purpose of subsumptive correlative is the demonstration<br>of the knowledge of the existence of reason in the subject.<br>The purpose of conclusion is to demonstrate the existence<br>of the thing to be proved in the subject.
        - **Item 67**:
          - **verse_number**: `67`
          - **original_text**: 10. The special condition for anumiti - linga parāmarśa:<br>स्वार्थानुमितिपरार्थानुमित्योः लिङ्गपरामर्श एव करणम् । तस्मात्<br>लिङ्गपरामर्शोऽनुमानम् ॥ १० ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 10. Consideration of the mark alone is the special<br>cause of inferential knowledge for oneself as well as<br>for others. Therefore, consideration of the mark is<br>inference.
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: अनुमितिकरणमाह - स्वार्थेति । ननु व्याप्तिस्मृतिपक्षधर्मताज्ञानाभ्यामेव अनुमितिसंभवे<br>विशिष्टपरामर्शः किमर्थमङ्गीकर्तव्य इति चेत् न, 'वह्निव्याप्यवानयम्' इति शाब्दपरामर्शस्थले<br>परामर्शस्यावश्यकतया लाघवेन सर्वत्र परामर्शस्यैव कारणत्वात् । लिङ्गं न करणम्,<br>अतीतादौ व्यभिचारात् । 'व्यापारवत्कारणं करणम्' इति मते परामर्शद्वारा व्याप्तिज्ञानं<br>करणम् । तज्जन्यत्वे सति तज्जन्यजनको व्यापारः । अनुमानमुपसंहरति- तस्मादिति ।
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>The Text describes the instrument of inferential knowledge<br>through words: svārtha....<br>Objection: Since inferential knowledge can be had by the<br>mere remembrance of invariable concomitance and by the<br>knowledge of the existence of reason in mountain, why should<br>one accept consideration of the mark with the adjunct of<br>the knowledge of invariable concomitance (as the special cause<br>of inferential knowledge)?<br>[93. Consideration of the mark lingaparāmarsa - (in the Text)<br>means trtīya-linga-parāmarsa, that is to say, the consideration derived<br>from the third mark. Then, naturally the question arises as to the<br>consideration of the other two marks. The knowledge of smoke in<br>hearth is the first consideration of the mark. The knowledge of smoke<br>in the subject is the second consideration of the mark. The knowledge<br>END_OF_PDF_PAGE_183<br>this mountain is smoky pervaded by fire is the third consideration<br>of the mark. This is also known as the consideration of the third<br>mark trtīyalinga-parāmarsa. The argument is that by having two<br>knowledges (1) of the invariable concomitance of smoke and fire<br>and (2) (by the knowledge) of the existence of smoke in the mountain<br>one can have the inferential knowledge: the mountain is fiery. Thus<br>the need for the assumption of the consideration of the third mark<br>(visista) to infer fire in a mountain is only a superfluity]<br>Reply: In cases where the subsumptive reflection, this<br>mountain is smoky as pervaded by fire is caused by mere<br>words (on hearing it from others, without actually perceiving<br>the existence of smoke in the mountain and also without<br>possessing the knowledge of invariable concomitance), one<br>has to take recourse to visista parāmarśa (to arrive at inferential<br>knowledge). So, keeping in view, the principle of parsimony<br>and brevity of expression, everywhere trtīya-linga-parāmarśa<br>is accepted as the special cause of inferential knowledge.<br>Mere mark is not the special cause of anumiti, because<br>of the deviation that would crop up (if we accept mark<br>as the special cause) in the inferential knowledge arising from<br>a reason which existed in the past or which is yet to come<br>into existence. It is the knowledge of mark that is the special<br>cause of inferential knowledge.<br>[94. On observation of sooted ceilings etc one can easily infer<br>the existence of smoke, as without smoke there would be no soot,<br>that is to say, soot is the effect of smoke. Through this inference<br>of smoke, one infers the past existence of fire like, mahānasah atīta<br>vahnimān, atīta dhūmavatvāt ie there existed fire in the hearth, because<br>of the existence of smoke, by applying vyāptijñāna, where smoke will<br>be, there will also be fire. In this inference no linga is perceived<br>to exist. We first infer the existence of linga by observing the soot,<br>and then, through the help of this lingajñāna, the existence of fire<br>is inferred. If linga is accepted as the special cause of inference,<br>then no inferential knowledge could arise in the absence of linga.<br>But here is an example where, even in the absence of linga inferential<br>knowledge arises by means of lingajñāna. Therefore, liiga is not<br>the special cause. This is with reference to the hetu existed in the<br>past. Again, a man, from within his room, hears peals of thunder.<br>On hearing the thunder roar, he infers the existence of clouds in<br>the sky though he does not actually perceive them, and proceeds<br>to infer that there would be rain. Here is a case wherein the inferential<br>knowledge arises without actually seeing the linga. It is only the<br>knowledge of the linga that helps to infer the future event of having<br>rain. Thus, liiga is not and cannot, for the above mentioned reasons,<br>be the special cause of inferential knowledge as without the liriga<br>also anumiti arises.]<br>The cause that is operative is the special cause. According<br>to this definition, the knowledge of the invariable concomitance<br>which has subsumptive reflection as its operation is the special<br>cause of inferential knowledge. Vyāpāra (operation) is that<br>which having been produced by any cause, becomes the cause<br>of the product which is to be produced by the cause (of<br>vyāpāra).[95. To explain: potter's stick is the special cause in the production<br>of a pot, because it has vyāpāra. Here the rotation of the potter's<br>wheel produced by the stick is the operation, and this is born of<br>that, and this vyāpāra produces the pot which is to be produced<br>by the stick. That is to say, stick is the special cause of jar by<br>means of its vyāpāra. Similarly, vyāptijñāna is the special cause of<br>inferential knowledge because it has subsumptive reflection as its vyāpāra.<br>Here subsumptive reflection is the vyāpāra of vyāptijnana because<br>it is produced by the latter, and parāmarśa produces inferential knowledge<br>which is to be produced by vyāptijñāna, ie vyāpti jñāna produces<br>parāmarśa, which in turn becomes the cause of anumiti. Vyāptijñāna<br>thus becomes the cause of anumiti by means of its vyāpāra, viz,<br>parāmarsa] The Author concludes the section on anumāna<br>with the words: Tasmāt.
        - **Item 68**:
          - **verse_number**: `68`
          - **original_text**: 11. Linga is threefold:<br>लिङ्गं त्रिविधम् । अन्वयव्यतिरेकि, केवलान्वयि, केवलव्यतिरेकि<br>चेति । अन्वयेन व्यतिरेकेण च व्याप्तिमदन्वयव्यतिरेकि । यथा<br>वह्नौ साध्ये धूमवत्त्वम् । यत्र धूमस्तत्राग्निर्यथा महानस<br>इत्यन्वयव्याप्तिः । यत्र वह्निर्नास्ति तत्र धूमोऽपि नास्ति यथा<br>महाहृद इति व्यतिरेकव्याप्तिः ॥ ११ ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 11. Mark is threefold: that which is (a) positive<br>and negative; (b) purely positive; (c) purely negative.<br>That which pervades both positively and negatively is<br>the positive-negative; eg smokiness when fire is the thing<br>to be proved. The proposition, where there is smoke,<br>there is fire, as in a hearth is the positive concomitance.<br>The proposition where there is the absence of fire, there<br>is the absence of smoke, as in a lake, is the negative<br>concomitance.
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: लिङ्गं विभजते — लिङ्गमिति ॥ अन्वयव्यतिरेकिणं लक्षयति — अन्वयेनेति ॥<br>हेतुसाध्ययोर्व्याप्तिरन्वयव्याप्तिः, तदभावयोव्र्व्याप्तिर्व्यतिरेकव्याप्तिः ।
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>Mark is divided by the Text: trividham. The Text defines<br>the positive-negative concomitance: anvayena.... The positive<br>concomitance is the invariable co-existence of the reason with<br>the thing to be proved. The negative concomitance is the<br>invariable absence of the reason wherever the thing to be<br>proved is absent.<br>[96. Vyāpti is twofold: positive concomitance and negative<br>concomitance. The concomitance between the thing to be proved<br>and the reason is the positive concomitance. The concomitance between<br>the absence of the thing to be proved and the absence of the reason,<br>like: 'where there is the absence of the thing to be proved there<br>is also the absence of reason' is negative concomitance. The reason<br>having both these concomitances is the positive-negative reason, eg<br>smoke. The reason having only positive concomitance is the purely<br>positive reason, eg a jar is nameable because of knowability. The<br>reason having only negative concomitance is purely negative reason,<br>eg earth differs from everything else because it possesses smell. This<br>is stated next by the Text]
        - **Item 69**:
          - **verse_number**: `69`
          - **original_text**: 12. Kevalānvayi linga:<br>अन्वयमात्रव्याप्तिकं केवलान्वयि । यथा घटः अभिधेयः प्रमेयत्वात्<br>पटवत् । अत्र प्रमेयत्वाभिधेयत्वयोः व्यतिरेकव्याप्तिर्नास्ति,<br>सर्वस्यापि प्रमेयत्वात् अभिधेयत्वाच्च ॥ १२ ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 12. Purely positive linga is that which has only<br>positive concomitance, eg jar is nameable because it<br>is knowable, like a cloth. Here, there is no negative<br>concomitance of knowability or nameability, as all things<br>are both knowable and nameable.
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: केवलान्वयिनो लक्षणमाह — अन्वयेति ॥ केवलान्वयिसाध्यकं लिङ्ग केवलान्वयि ।<br>(वृत्तिमत्) अत्यन्ताभावाप्रतियोगित्वं केवलान्वयित्वम् । ईश्वरप्रमाविषयत्वं सर्वपदाभिधेयत्वं<br>च सर्वत्रास्तीति व्यतिरेकाभावः ।
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>The Text defines the purely positive reason: anvaya....<br>The mark that has only positive concomitance is the purely<br>positive reason. Kevalānvayitva is never being the<br>counter-correlative of absolute non-existence. This is shown<br>through the example: jar is nameable because it is knowable.<br>Since everything is knowable to the Lord and also is nameable,<br>as everything is included in the word all which is the collectivc<br>term for everything that is nameable, negative concomitance<br>does not exist anywhere.
        - **Item 70**:
          - **verse_number**: `70`
          - **original_text**: 13. Kevalavyatireki linga:<br>व्यतिरेकमात्रव्याप्तिकं केवलव्यतिरेकि, यथा पृथिवीतरेभ्यो भिद्यते<br>गन्धवत्त्वात् । यदितरेभ्यो न भिद्यते न तद्गन्धवत् यथा जलम् ।<br>न चेयं तथा । तस्मान्न तथेति । अत्र यद्गन्धवत् तदितरभिन्नम्<br>इत्यन्वयदृष्टान्तो नास्ति, पृथिवीमात्रस्य पक्षत्वात् ॥ १३ ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 13. The purely negative reason is that which has<br>a negative concomitance only, eg earth differs from the<br>rest because it has smell. Whichever does not differ<br>from the rest has no smell in it, eg water. But this<br>earth is not so (because there is no absence of smell).<br>Therefore, it is not so. Here, there is no positive example<br>like whichever has smell is different from others, because<br>the whole earth constitutes the subject here (as all<br>varieties of products and transformations of earth<br>constitute the subject here).
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: केवलव्यतिरेकिणो लक्षणमाह — व्यतिरेकेति ॥ तदुदाहरति-यथेति । नन्वितरभेदः प्रसिद्धो वा न वा । आद्ये यत्र प्रसिद्धस्तत्र हेतुसत्त्वे अन्वयित्वम्, असत्त्वे असाधारण्यम् । द्वितीये साध्यज्ञानाभावात्कथं तद्विशिष्टानुमितिः । विशेषणज्ञानाभावे विशिष्टज्ञानानुदयात्प्रतियोगिज्ञानाभावाद्वयतिरेकव्याप्तिज्ञानमपि न स्यादिति चेत् न । जलादित्रयोदशान्योन्याभावानां त्रयोदशसु प्रत्येकं प्रसिद्धानां मेलनं पृथिव्यां साध्यते । तत्र त्रयोदशत्वावच्छिन्नभेदात्मक - साध्यस्यैकाधिकरणवृत्तित्वाभावान्नान्वयित्वासाधारण्ये । प्रत्येकाधिकरणप्रसिद्धया साध्यविशिष्टानुमितिः व्यतिरेकव्याप्तिनिरूपणं चेति ।
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>The characteristic mark of purely negative reason is given in the Text: vyatireka.... It is further illustrated, pṛthivī....<br>Objection: Is difference from the rest previously known, or not known? If the former, (ie if the difference from the rest is known to exist somewhere other than earth), then, the reason either co-exists with it or not; if the reason co-exists, then there is a positive concomitance. (Therefore, the hetu cannot be called a purely negative one); if the reason does not co-exist, then there occurs the defect of asādhāranya. In the latter event, (ie if itarabheda is not known), then the knowledge of the thing to be proved being unknown, how can there be inferential knowledge, characterised by itself, because of the maxim: a qualified thing cannot be known without the previous knowledge of the qualifier?? Also, since the knowledge of negation depends on the knowledge of its counter-entity, the negation of difference from the rest also is unknown; hence there can be no knowledge of negative concomitance.<br>Reply: It is known that the thirteen mutual negations of water etc reside severally in each of the thirteen things like water etc at a time. And the aggregate (ie all the thirteen mutual negations) is shown to exist at one and the same time in earth. Therefore, due to the absence of the simultaneous existence of the thirteen mutual negations on any one thing we cannot know whether the hetu exists there or not. And, having thus no knowledge of the hetu, positive or negative, we can neither call it anvayī or asādhāranya. (This refutes<br>the first objection. The following argument refutes the second<br>objection). Difference from the rest which is the thing to<br>be proved is already known to exist severally in (the thirteen<br>as shown above); hence, it is possible to have inferential<br>knowledge as characterized by the sādhya as pervaded by<br>the negation of sadhana is determined.<br>[97. The idea is this: The inference pṛthivī itarabhedavatī is<br>characterized by the sādhya. If the sādhya itself is unknown, how<br>can such a knowledge arise? For instance, one cannot know a daṇḍin<br>unless one first knows what a daṇḍa is]
        - **Item 71**:
          - **verse_number**: `71`
          - **original_text**: 14. Subject (paksa):<br>सन्दिग्धसाध्यवान् पक्षः । यथा धूमवत्त्त्वे हेतौ पर्वतः ॥ १४ ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 14. Subject is that on which the thing to be proved<br>is suspected, eg a mountain when smokiness or smoke<br>is the reason.
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: पक्षलक्षणमाह सन्दिग्धेति ॥ ननु श्रवणानन्तरभाविमननस्थले अव्याप्तिः । तत्र<br>वेदवाक्यैरात्मनो निश्चितत्वेन सन्देहाभावात् । किञ्च प्रत्यक्षेऽपि वहनौ<br>यत्रेच्छयानुमितिस्तत्राव्याप्तिरिति चेत् न, उक्तपक्षताश्रयत्वस्य पक्षलक्षणत्वात् ।
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>The Text defines the subject: sandigdha....<br>Objection: Here, the defect of avyapti occurs in reflection<br>(like, the soul is different from the body etc) because, the<br>knowledge (of the soul as different from the body etc) is<br>ascertained by hearing it (from the preceptor. Hence here<br>is the absence of doubt in the paksa. So, here is avyāpti<br>of paksalaksana in manana). Also, the defect of avyapti occurs<br>in instances when one wishes to infer fire even after having<br>directly perceived it. (That is, the existence of fire is determined<br>by perception; so, here there is no doubt with regard to<br>its existence in the paksa. So avyāpti occurs).<br>Reply: This objection is untenable; because pakṣatā is to<br>be interpreted in the manner explained earlier (to avoid ativyāpti<br>in samsayottara pratyaksa). Paksatā is the absence of the<br>indubious knowledge of the thing to be proved accompanied<br>by an absence of desire to establish an already ascertained<br>Sādhya.<br>[98. The thirteen bhedas are: 8 dravyas (other than the dravya<br>where the itarabheda resides,) guṇa, karma, sāmānya, viśeṣa and<br>samavāya. When predicated severally, the difficulty that would arise<br>if itarabheda is taken as a unit, would not arise; what is predicated<br>is that the collection of thirteen itarabhedas are in earth and these<br>thirteen itarabhedas are predicated severally. The fourteen bhāvapadārthas<br>imply thirteen reciprocal differences (itarabhedas) inasmuch as any<br>one of them is the anuyogin (seat) of these itarabhedas. If water<br>is taken as the subject, in it reside the thirteen bhedas except jalabheda;<br>similarly, when light is taken as the subject, in it reside thirteen<br>bhedas except tejobheda. Thus jalabheda is known in tejas and tejobheda<br>is known in jala. In this way all the 14 bhedas are prasiddha severally<br>and not as a unit]
        - **Item 72**:
          - **verse_number**: `72`
          - **original_text**: 15. Similar instance (sapaksa):<br>निश्चितसाध्यवान् सपक्षः, यथा तत्रैव महानसम् ॥ १५ ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 15. The subject in which the existence of the thing<br>to be proved is known for certain is the similar instance;<br>eg a hearth in the same case.
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: सपक्षलक्षणमाह निश्चितेति ॥
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>The Text defines similar instance niścita....<br>[99. In the hearth the existence of fire is ascertained when we<br>say, mahānasam vahnimat. This forms the sapakṣa for the inference<br>in which smoke is the reason]
        - **Item 73**:
          - **verse_number**: `73`
          - **original_text**: 16. Contrary instance (Vipaksa):<br>निश्चितसाध्याऽभाववान् विपक्षः । यथा तत्रैव महाहदः ॥ १६ ॥

        - **Item 74**:
          - **verse_number**: `74`
          - **original_text**: 17. Defective reason (hetvābhāsa):<br>सव्यभिचारविरुद्धसत्प्रतिपक्षासिद्धबाधिताः पञ्च हेत्वा-<br>भासाः ॥ १७ ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 17. The defective reasons are five (a) straying;<br>(b) adverse; (c) antithetical; (d) unestablished; and<br>(e) stultified.
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: एवं सद्धेतून्निरूप्य असद्धेतून्निरूपयितुं विभजते — सव्यभिचारेति \|\| अनुमितिप्रति-<br>बन्धकयथार्थज्ञानविषयत्वं हेत्वाभासत्वम् ।
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>Having first explained the characteristic marks of faultless<br>reasons, now, the Text deals with defective reasons by dividing<br>them into straying reason etc (with a view to indicate that<br>these reasons should not be employed in an inference). Defective<br>reasoning consists in being the subject of valid apprehension<br>which prevents inferential knowledge.
        - **Item 75**:
          - **verse_number**: `75`
          - **original_text**: 18. Savyabhicāra hetu is threefold:<br>सव्यभिचारः अनैकान्तिकः । स त्रिविधः<br>साधारणासाधारणानुपसंहारिभेदात् ॥ १८ ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 18. The straying reason is one that is known also<br>as the deviating. It is of three kinds due to its division<br>into common, uncommon, and non-exclusive.
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: सव्यभिचारं विभजते स त्रिविध इति ॥
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>The Text divides the straying reason: sa irividhaḥ....
        - **Item 76**:
          - **verse_number**: `76`
          - **original_text**: 19. Sādhāraņa savyabhicāra hetu:<br>तत्र साध्याभाववद्वृत्तिः साधारणः अनैकान्तिकः, यथा पर्वतो<br>वह्निमान् प्रमेयत्वात् इति । प्रमेयत्वस्य वह्नयभाववति हदे<br>विद्यमानत्वात् ॥ १९ ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 19. Of these, the reason which is present in a<br>place where there is the absence of the thing to be<br>proved, is the common strayer reason; eg the argument<br>mountain is fiery, because, it is knowable; here, knowability<br>exists in a lake also where there is the absence of<br>fire.
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: साधारणं लक्षयति तत्रेति ॥ उदाहरति- यथेति ॥
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>The Text defines the common strayer reason; tatra....<br>It gives an example: yathā....<br>[101. In this argument fire is the thing to be proved. Knowability<br>is the reason. But the reason is found to exist in a lake also where<br>there is the absence of fire. Thus, reason exists in a place where<br>there is the thing to be proved, it also exists in a place where<br>there is the absence of the thing to be proved. This obstructs the<br>knowledge of the invariable concomitance: wherever there is knowability<br>there is also fire. So, this reason is defective and is known as the<br>common strayer reason]
        - **Item 77**:
          - **verse_number**: `77`
          - **original_text**: 20. Asādhārana savyabhicāra hetu:<br>सर्वसपक्षविपक्षव्यावृत्तः पक्षमात्रवृत्तिः असाधारणः । 'यथा शब्दो<br>नित्यः शब्दत्वात् इति । शब्दत्वं हि सर्वेभ्यो नित्येभ्योऽनित्येभ्यश्च<br>व्यावृत्तं शब्दमात्रवृत्तिः ॥ २० ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 20. The uncommon strayer reason is that which<br>is found only in the subject while not being present<br>in any similar and contrary instances; eg sound is eternal<br>because of soundness. Soundness exists only in sound<br>and is absent in all eternal and non-eternal things.
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: असाधारणं लक्षयति — सर्वेति ॥
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>The Text defines the uncommon strayer: sarva....<br>[102. In the argument: sound is eternal because of soundness, eternality<br>is the thing to be proved and soundness is the reason. This reason<br>does not exist in its similar instances like space etc nor in its contrary<br>instances like pot etc]
        - **Item 78**:
          - **verse_number**: `78`
          - **original_text**: 21. Anupasamhāri savyabhicāra hetu:<br>अन्वयव्यतिरेकदृष्टान्तरहितोऽनुपसंहारी । यथा सर्वमनित्यं<br>प्रमेयत्वादिति । अत्र सर्वस्यापि पक्षत्वात् दृष्टान्तो<br>नास्ति ॥ २१ ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 21. The non-exclusive strayer reason is that which<br>has neither positive instance nor negative instance, as<br>in the argument, everything is non-eternal because it is<br>knowable. Here, since everything is treated as the subject<br>no example is available to support the inference.
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: अनुपसंहारिणो लक्षणमाह — अन्वयेति ॥
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>The Text defines the non-exclusive strayer; anvaya....<br>[103. The difference between the uncommon strayer and<br>non-exclusive strayer reasons is that in the former both similar and<br>contrary instances are available though the reason is not present in<br>paksa; in the latter both similar and contrary instances are absent.<br>The term sarva is so wide and inclusive that nothing is left to<br>serve as a similar or contrary instance]
        - **Item 79**:
          - **verse_number**: `79`
          - **original_text**: 22. Viruddha hetu:<br>साध्याभावव्याप्तो हेतुर्विरुद्धः । यथा शब्दो नित्यः कृतकत्वादिति ।<br>कृतकत्वं हि नित्यत्वाभावेनाऽनित्यत्वेन व्याप्तम् ॥ २२ ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 22. The self-contradicting reason is that which is<br>pervaded by the negation of the thing to be proved<br>eg the argument, sound is eternal because it is produced.<br>Here, producibility is pervasive of non-eternality which<br>is of the nature of the negation of eternality.
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: विरुद्धं लक्षयति — साध्येति ॥
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>The Text defines the self-contradicting reason: sādhya....<br>[104. In the inference, sound is eternal, because it is produced,<br>sound is the paksa, eternality is the thing to be proved, and producibility<br>is the reason. But producibility never co-exists with eternality as<br>it is invariably concomitant with non-eternality. Thus, when a reason<br>which is offered to establish a thing to be proved actually serves<br>to prove the very negation of it, we call it a viruddha hetu]
        - **Item 80**:
          - **verse_number**: `80`
          - **original_text**: 23. Satpratipakṣa hetu:<br>यस्य साध्याभावसाधकं हेत्वन्तरं विद्यते स सत्प्रतिपक्षः ।<br>यथा शब्दो नित्यः श्रावणत्वात् शब्दत्ववत् । शब्दोऽनित्यः<br>कार्यत्वात् घटवत् ॥ २३ ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 23. The antithetical reason is that of which there<br>exists another reason capable of proving the negation<br>of the thing to be proved, as in the argument: sound<br>is eternal, because it is audible, like soundness; and sound<br>is non-eternal, because it is a product, like a jar.<br>[105. In the counter argument, sound is non-eternal. because it<br>is product, like a jar, producibility is the counter reason that proves<br>the negation of eternality of sound which is the thing to be proved<br>in the earlier inference. So, in the argument, sound is eternal because<br>it is audible, the reason audibility admits of another reason capable<br>of disproving the sadhya established by it. So, it is a defective<br>reason]
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: सत्प्रतिपक्षं लक्षयति — यस्येति ॥
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>The Text defines the antithetical reason: yasya....
        - **Item 81**:
          - **verse_number**: `81`
          - **original_text**: 24. Asiddha hetu is threefold:<br>असिद्धस्त्रिविधः — आश्रयासिद्धः, स्वरूपासिद्धो<br>व्याप्यत्वासिद्धश्वेति ॥ २४ ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 24. The unestablished reason is of three kinds:<br>(a) unestablished in respect of its substratum;<br>(b) unestablished in respect of itself; and<br>(c) unestablished in respect of its concomitance.

        - **Item 82**:
          - **verse_number**: `82`
          - **original_text**: 25. Āśrayāsiddha:<br>आश्रयासिद्धो यथा गगनारविन्दं सुरभि अरविन्दत्वात्<br>सरोजारविन्दवत् । अत्र गगनारविन्दमाश्रयः स च<br>नास्त्येव ॥ २५ ॥

        - **Item 83**:
          - **verse_number**: `83`
          - **original_text**: 26. Svarūpāsiddha:<br>स्वरूपासिद्धो यथा शब्दो गुणश्चाक्षुषत्वात् । अत्र चाक्षुषत्वं<br>शब्दं नास्ति शब्दस्य श्रावणत्वात् ॥ २६ ॥
