        - **Item 84**:
          - **verse_number**: `84`
          - **original_text**: 27. Vyāpyatvāsiddha:<br>सोपाधिको हेतुः व्याप्यत्वासिद्धः । साध्यव्यापकत्वे सति<br>साधनाव्यापकत्वं उपाधिः । साध्यसमानाधिकरणात्यन्ताभावा-<br>प्रतियोगित्वं साध्यव्यापकत्वम् । साधनवन्निष्ठात्यन्ताभाव-<br>प्रतियोगित्वं साधनाव्यापकत्वम् । पर्वतो धूमवान्वह्निमत्वादित्यत्र<br>आर्द्रन्धनसंयोग उपाधिः । तथाहि । यत्र धूमस्तत्रार्द्रन्धनसंयोग<br>इति साध्यव्यापकता । यत्र वह्निस्तत्रार्द्रन्धनसंयोगो नास्ति<br>अयोगोलके आर्मेन्धनसंयोगाभावादिति साधनाव्यापकता । एवं<br>साध्यव्यापकत्वे सति साधनाव्यापकत्वादार्द्रन्धनसंयोग उपाधिः ।<br>सोपाधिकत्वात् वह्निमत्त्वं व्याप्यत्वासिद्धम् ॥ २७ ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 27. The reason associated with an adventitious<br>condition is known as unestablished in respect of its<br>own pervasion; adventitious condition is that which<br>pervades the thing to be proved but is not pervasive<br>of the reason. (That which invariably co-exists with<br>sādhya is sādhyavyāpaka; that which does not co-exist<br>in some place with sadhana is sādhana avyāpaka). Not<br>having counter-correlative of its absolute negation while<br>co-existing with the thing to be proved is being pervasive<br>of the thing to be proved. Having the counter-entity<br>of absolute negation co-existing with reason is being<br>non-pervasive of reason. In the argument: Mountain<br>is smoky, because it has fire, contact with wet fuel<br>is the upādhi. It is pervasive of the thing to be proved.<br>(ie smoke) because of the concomitance, wherever there<br>is smoke there is contact with wet fuel. But there is<br>no contact with wet fuel wherever there is fire; for<br>example, in a red hot iron-ball there is fire, though<br>contact with wet fuel is not there. Thus the adventitious<br>condition is non-pervasive of the reason. In like manner,<br>contact with wet fuel is the adventitious condition in<br>the present instance because it is pervasive of the thing<br>to be proved while not being pervasive of the reason<br>Therefore, fire which is the reason in the argument
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: व्याप्यत्वासिद्धस्य लक्षणमाह सोपाधिक इति ॥ उपाधेर्लक्षणमाह साध्येति ॥<br>उपाधिश्चतुर्विधः केवलसाध्यव्यापकः, पक्षधर्मावच्छिन्नसाध्यव्यापकः, साधनावच्छिन्नसा-<br>ध्यव्यापकः, उदासीनधर्मावच्छिन्नसाध्यव्यापकश्चेति । आद्यः आर्द्रन्धनसंयोगः । द्वितीयो<br>यथा वायुः प्रत्यक्षः प्रत्यक्षरपर्शाश्रयत्वात् इत्यत्र बहिर्दव्यत्वावच्छिन्न-<br>प्रत्यक्षत्वव्यापकमुद्भूतरूपवत्त्वम् । तृतीयो यथा प्रागभावो विनाशी जन्यत्वादित्यत्र<br>जन्यत्वावच्छिन्नानित्यत्वव्यापकं भावत्वम् । चतुर्थो यथा प्रागभावो विनाशी<br>पमेयत्वात् इत्यत्र जन्यत्वावच्छिन्नानित्यत्वव्यापकं भावत्वम् ।
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>The Text defines the reason unestablished in respect of<br>itself: sopādhiko.... Upādhi (adventitious condition) is of four<br>types: (1) Pervasive of sādhya and non-pervasive of reason;<br>(2) that which is pervasive of sādhya delimited by the property<br>of residing in paksa and is non-pervasive of reason; (3) that<br>which is pervasive of sādhya delimited by the property of<br>residing in the reason and is non-pervasive of reason, and<br>(4) that which is pervasive of sādhya delimited by a property<br>which has no concern either of paksa or reason and is<br>non-pervasive of reason. The example for the first kind of<br>upādhi is contact with wet fuel. Let us now take the second<br>kind, and illustrate it with the following example: Air is perceptible<br>because of its being the substratum of touch caused by perception.<br>Here touch, ie the state of being the substratum of touch,<br>is sopādhika (with the defect of upādhi). Manifested colour<br>is the upādhi in this instance; because it is pervasive of<br>the sādhya, viz, perceptibility. Indeed it is so; wherever there<br>is perceptibility delimited by substanceness (the property of<br>residing in the paksa) there is manifested colour. Here manifested<br>colour is pervasive of sādhya, viz perceptibility. It should<br>not be argued that since manifested colour is not found to<br>exist in air in spite of its being possessed of perceptibility<br>and substanceness in it, manifested colour cannot be held<br>as upādhi; because it is not possible to include here air<br>in the category of substances possessing perceptibility inasmuch<br>as air is the paksa here, in which perceptibility has yet to<br>be ascertained. Thus, there is touch in earth, water and tejas.<br>Accordingly, there will also be manifested colour in the above<br>three substances. Since by substanceness only the property<br>residing in substances other than Atma is meant, the inclusion<br>of Atma in the category of substance becomes excluded. So,<br>the possible argument, there is perceptibility in Atma but there<br>is no manifested colour is being averted. Thus, a part of<br>the definition of upādhi, viz that which is pervasive of sādhya<br>delimited by the property of residing in paksa is established<br>in manifested colour. The other part of the definition viz<br>non-pervasive of reason, is found to be in the manifested colour<br>thus: wherever there is touch there need not be manifested<br>colour (eg there is touch in the Air, but there is no manifold<br>colour) in it. (It should be noted here that the absence of<br>manifested colour is not under dispute in the paksa, viz air).<br>That which becomes vyāpaka even to that which is vyāpaka<br>to reason becomes vyāpya to the reason is the upādhi and<br>a reason with this defect of upadhi becomes defective.<br>The third kind of upādhi is found in the argument: Antecedent<br>negation is destructible because of being produced. Here, bhāvatvam<br>(Being-ness) is the upadhi. Where there is sādhya, viz<br>non-eternality, delimited by the property (ie the state of being<br>created) residing in the reason viz, effect (production), there<br>is bhāvatvam. There is non-eternality delimited by the property,<br>viz, the state of being created, as in a pot and the like.<br>But, where there is the reason janyatvam in dhvamsa where<br>there is bhāvatvam. Thus, the reason in the above argument<br>is sopādhika, and is defective.<br>The fourth kind of upādhi is found in the argument:<br>Antecedent negation is destructible because of its being knowable.<br>Here too bhāvatvam is upādhi. Where there is sādhya delimited<br>by the property janyatvam (the property that belongs neither<br>to paksa nor to reason) there is bhāvatvam. But where there<br>is the reason knowability, eg in negation, there is not bhāvatvam.<br>Thus, the reason being sopādhika, it is defective.
        - **Item 85**:
          - **verse_number**: `85`
          - **original_text**: 28. Bādhita hetu:<br>यस्य साध्याभावः प्रमाणान्तरेण निश्चितः स बाधितः । यथा<br>वह्निरनुष्णो द्रव्यत्वात् जलवत् । अत्रानुष्णत्वं साध्यं तदभाव<br>उष्णत्वं स्पर्शनप्रत्यक्षेण गुह्यत इति बाधितत्वम् ॥ २८ ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 28. The contradicted reason is one where the<br>negation of the thing to be proved is established by<br>another proof, eg fire is not-hot, because it is a substance,<br>like water. Here, non-hotness is the thing to be proved.<br>But hotness, the negation of non-hotness, is cognised<br>through tactile perception. Therefore, the above reason<br>is known as the contradicted reason.
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: बाधितस्य लक्षणमाह - यस्येति ।। अत्र बाधस्य ग्राह्याभावनिश्चयत्वेन, सत्प्रतिपक्षस्य<br>विरोधिज्ञानसामग्रीत्वेन साक्षादनुमितिप्रतिबन्धकत्वम् । इतरेषां तु परामर्शप्रतिबन्धकत्वम् ।<br>तत्रापि साधारणस्याव्यभिचाराभावरूपतया, विरुद्धस्य सामानाधिकरण्याभावतया<br>व्याप्यत्वासिद्धस्य विशिष्टव्याप्त्यभावतया, असाधारणानुपसंहारिणोः व्याप्तिसंशयाधायकत्वेन<br>व्याप्तिज्ञानप्रतिबन्धकत्वम्, आश्रयासिद्धिस्वरूपासिद्धयोः पक्षधर्मताज्ञानप्रतिबन्धकत्वम् ।<br>उपाधिस्तु व्यभिचारज्ञानद्वारा व्याप्तिज्ञानप्रतिबन्धकः । सिद्धसाधनं तु पक्षताविघटकतया<br>आश्रयासिद्धावन्तर्भवतीति प्राञ्चः । निग्रहस्थानान्तरमिति नवीनाः ॥
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>The Text defines the contradicted reason: yasya.... The<br>stultified and the antithetical reasons directly obstruct inferential<br>knowledge: the first, through the determination of the<br>non-existence of the thing to be proved; the second, through<br>the entire causal apparatus bringing about the adverse cognition<br>(by determining the negation of the thing to be proved).<br>In other cases, inference is obstructed owing to the obstruction<br>to subsumptive reflection. Even among them, inferential<br>knowledge is prevented owing to the prevention of the knowledge<br>of invariable concomitance due to the obstruction caused by<br>such counter agents as (a) sādhārana in which there is the<br>absence of the absence of inconsistency; (b) viruddha in which<br>there is the absence of predicative correlation of the thing<br>to be proved and the reason; (c) vyāpyatvāsiddha, in which<br>there is the absence of determinate concomitance; and (d)<br>asādhāraņa and anupasamharin in which there is the presence<br>of doubtful invariable concomitance; in āśrayāsiddha and<br>svarūpāsiddha, there exists obstruction to the knowledge of<br>the presence of reason in the subject. But upādhi prevents<br>inferential knowledge by counteracting the knowledge of<br>invariable concomitance through the knowledge of inconsistency.<br>Naiyāyikas belonging to the traditional school are of the opinion<br>that the re-establishment of an already proved sādhya like:<br>jar is non-eternal, because it is a product, gets included in<br>āśrayāsiddha itself on account of the destruction of paksatā;<br>but the modern Naiyāyikas are of the opinion that it is included<br>in vulnerable points (nigrahasthāna), which cause defeat in<br>a debate.
        - **Item 86**:
          - **verse_number**: `86`
          - **original_text**: SECTION VI<br>UPAMĀNA - ASSIMILATIVE ANALOGY<br>उपमितिकरणमुपमानम् । संज्ञासंज्ञिसम्बन्धज्ञानमुपमितिः ।<br>तत्करणं सादृश्यज्ञानम् । अतिदेशवाक्यार्थस्मरणमवान्तर<br>व्यापारः । तथा हि कश्चिद्गवयशब्दार्थमजानन् कुतश्चित्<br>आरण्यकपुरुषाद्गोसदृशो गवय इति श्रुत्वा वनं गतो वाक्यार्थं<br>स्मरन् गोसदृशं पिण्डं पश्यति । तदनन्तरमसौ गवयशब्दवाच्य<br>इत्युपमितिरुत्पद्यते ॥ १ ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 1. Analogy is the instrument of assimilative<br>knowledge. It is the knowledge of the relation existing<br>between a name and the object denoted by it. The<br>knowledge of similarity is its immediate special cause;<br>the remembrance of the meaning of a former direction<br>conveying similarity between two objects, is the<br>intermediary operation; this may be exemplified thus:<br>a person who is ignorant of the object denoted by<br>the word gavaya learns from a certain forester that<br>the gavaya is similar to a cow. Subsequently, going<br>to the forest, he sees the strange animal similar to<br>a cow, and remembers the declaration. Then arises<br>in him the knowledge: This is the animal denoted by<br>the word gavaya.
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: उपमानं लक्षयति — उपमितिकरणमिति ॥
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>The Text defines analogy: upamiti.... Annambhatta next<br>takes up for discussion upamāna which is the third of the<br>four pramāņas accepted by the Nyāya-Vaiśeṣika School. Upamiti<br>is the knowledge of relation between a term and the object<br>it stands for. The special condition under which upamiti arises<br>is the knowledge of similarity, which rises due to a statement<br>such as: gavaya is like a cow. When this knowledge is similar<br>to the knowledge arising from memory of what he had heard<br>before, (which is the intermediary vyāpara) one has upamiti.<br>In other words, upamiti is just the perceptual knowledge<br>of similarity.
        - **Item 87**:
          - **verse_number**: `87`
          - **original_text**: SECTION VII<br>SABDA - VALID VERBAL TESTIMONY<br>आप्तवाक्यं शब्दः । आप्तस्तु, यथार्थवक्ता । वाक्यं पदसमूहः ।<br>यथा गामानयेति । शक्तं पदम् । अस्मात्पदात् अयमर्थो बोद्धव्य<br>इतीश्वरसंकेतः शक्तिः ॥ १ ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 1.  Valid verbal testimony is a sentence spoken by<br>a trustworthy person. A trustworthy person is one who<br>speaks of things as they exist in reality. Sentence is<br>a group of words, like: Bring a cow. Word is that<br>which has significative potency. Significative potency is<br>the desire of God (and also of jīva) that a certain.<br>concept be understood from a certain word.
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: शब्दं लक्षयति - आप्तेति ॥ आप्तं लक्षयति - आप्तस्त्विति ॥ वाक्यलक्षणमाह<br>वाक्यमिति ॥ पदलक्षणमाह - शक्तमिति ॥ अर्थस्मृत्यनुकूलपदपदार्थसंबन्धः<br>शक्तिः । सा च पदार्थान्तरमिति मीमांसकाः । तन्निरासार्थमाह - अस्मादिति ॥<br>डित्थादीनामिव घटादीनामपि संकेत एव शक्तिः न तु पदार्थान्तरमित्यर्थः । ननु<br>गवादिपदानां जातावेव शक्तिः, विशेषणतया जातेः प्रथममुपस्थितत्वात् । व्यक्तिलाभस्तु<br>आक्षेपादिति केचित् । तत् न, गामानयेत्यादौ वृद्धव्यवहारेण सर्वत्रानयनादेर्व्यक्तावेव<br>संभवेन, जातिविशिष्टव्यक्तावेव शक्तिकल्पनात् । शक्तिग्रहश्च वृद्धव्यवहारेण । व्युत्पित्सुर्बालो<br>'गामानय' इत्युत्तमवृद्धवाक्यश्रवणानन्तरं मध्यमवृद्धस्य प्रवृत्तिमुपलभ्य गवानयनं च<br>दृष्ट्वा मध्यमवृद्धप्रवृत्तिजनकज्ञानस्यान्वयव्यतिरेकाभ्यां वाक्यजन्यत्वं निश्चित्य 'अश्वमानय<br>गां बधान' इति वाक्यान्तरे आवापोद्वापाभ्यां गोपदस्य गोत्वविशिष्ट शक्तिः, अश्वपदस्य<br>अश्वत्वविशिष्ट शक्तिरिति व्युत्पद्यते । ननु सर्वत्र कार्यपरत्वाद्वयवहारस्य कार्यपरवाक्य<br>एव व्युत्पत्तिर्न सिद्धपर इति चेत् न । 'काश्यां त्रिभुवनतिलको भूपतिरास्ते' इत्यादौ<br>सिद्धेऽपि व्यवहारात्, 'विकसितपद्म मधुकरस्तिष्ठति' इत्यादौ<br>प्रसिद्धपदसमभिव्याहारात्सिद्धेऽपि मधुकरादिव्युत्पत्तिदर्शनाच्च । लक्षणापि शब्दवृत्तिः ।<br>शक्यसंबन्धो लक्षणा । गङ्गायां घोष इत्यत्र गङ्गापदवाच्यप्रवाहसंबन्धादेव तीरोपस्थितौ<br>तीरऽपि शक्तिर्न कल्प्यते । सैन्धवादौ लवणाश्वयोः परस्परसंबन्धाभावान्नानाशक्तिकल्पनम् ।<br>लक्षणा त्रिविधा जहलक्षणा, अजहलक्षणा, जहदजहलक्षणा चेति । यत्र<br>वाच्यार्थस्यान्वयाभावः तत्र जहलक्षणा । यथा मञ्चाः क्रोशन्तीति । यत्र<br>वाच्यार्थस्याप्यन्वयः, तत्र अजहदिति । यथा छत्रिणो गच्छन्तीति । यत्र वाच्यैकदेशत्यागेन<br>एकदेशान्वयः, तत्र जहदजहदिति । यथा तत्त्वमसीति । गौण्यपि लक्षणैव<br>लक्ष्यमाणगुणसंबन्धस्वरूपा यथा अग्निर्माणवक इति । व्यञ्चनापि शक्तिलक्षणान्तर्भूता,<br>शब्दशक्तिमूला अर्थशक्तिमूला च । अनुमानादिना अन्यथासिद्धा ।<br>तात्पर्यानुपपत्तिर्लक्षणाबीजम् । तत्प्रतीतीच्छयोच्चरितत्वं तात्पर्यम् । तात्पर्यज्ञानञ्च<br>वाक्यार्थज्ञाने हेतुः नानार्थानुरोधात् । प्रकरणादिकं तात्पर्यग्राहकम् । द्वारमित्यादौ पिधेहीति<br>शब्दाध्याहारः । ननु अर्थज्ञानार्थत्वाच्छब्दस्यार्थमविज्ञाय शब्दाध्याहारासंभवादर्थाध्याहार<br>एव युक्त इति चेत् न । पदविशेषजन्यपदार्थोपस्थितेः शाब्दज्ञाने हेतुत्वात् । अन्यथा<br>'घटः कर्मत्वमानयनं कृतिः' इत्यत्रापि शाब्दज्ञानप्रसङ्गात् । पङ्कजादिपदेषु योगरूढिः ।<br>अवयवशक्तिर्योगः । समुदायशक्ती रूढिः । नियतपद्मत्वादिज्ञानार्थं समुदायशक्तिः । अन्यथा<br>कुमुदेऽपि प्रयोगप्रसङ्गात् । 'इतरान्विते शक्तिः' इति प्राभाकराः । अन्वयस्य वाक्यार्थतया<br>भानसम्भवादन्वयांशेऽपि शक्तिर्न कल्पनीया इति गौतमीयाः ।
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>The Text beginning with Apta... defines valid verbal<br>testimony. The Text Saktam... defines the characteristic of<br>Word. Significative potency is the relation of a word and<br>the object meant by that word which is always favourable<br>in reviving the memory of that object. The Mīmāmsakas are<br>of the opinion that significative potency is an independent<br>padārtha. To refute this argument of the Mīmāmsakas, the<br>Text says, Asmāt... etc.<br>The implication here is that śakti is not a distinct padārtha,<br>since śakti is considered as desire (which comes under the<br>category of quality). The śakti of things like pots etc is<br>in sanketa only, like the words Dittha etc (where the śakti<br>is in the persons indicated by these names).<br>Objection: The Sakti in words like cow etc is apprehended<br>only in the generic attribute, because of the immediate<br>apprehension of the notion of generic attribute inasmuch as<br>it is the attribute of the qualified object cow. The individual<br>(as qualified by the Jāti) is apprehended by necessary implication<br>(ie by means of Arthāpatti pramāna). <br>[106. A lot of controversy has arisen as to what is primarily<br>signified by the sakti of a pada, viz, whether the sakti of a word<br>signifies (a) the jati only; or, (b) the jātiviśişta vyakti, or (c) the<br>ākṛti (the form)? According to the Bhatta Mimamsakas and the Advaita<br>Vedantins, the sakti cf a word denotes only the generic attribute.<br>That is to say, the sakti in the word cow signifies the generic attribute<br>of cow. viz gotva. If the word were to mean an individual, then,<br>it must have as many śaktis as there are individuals meant by that<br>word. Thus the sakti of a word would be infinite corresponding<br>to the infinity of individuals. This, however, militates against the<br>general law of laghava (parsimony) that one word should have only<br>one primary meaning. Also, without the knowledge of the class-attribute<br>the apprehension of an individual is impossible. The apprehension<br>of an individual arises along with the knowledge of the corresponding<br>generic attribute, as they are inseparable. Thus the primary meaning<br>of a word is with regard to the genera and the apprehension of<br>the individuals takes place subsequently by means of arthapatti pramāṇa.<br>The stock example cited is: dandīpurusah - a man with a stick.<br>It is a well-known maxim that without the prior knowledge of the<br>attribute, the knowledge of the individual as determined by that attribute,<br>is impossible. Unless one has knowledge of the stick, one cannot<br>arrive at the knowledge. man with a stick. This proposition obviously<br>leads one to admit the need of arthapatti pramāṇa in order to apprehend<br>an individual as determined by a particular adjunct. Similarly, the<br>śakti in the word cow signifies only the generic attribute and it is<br>only by means of arthapatti that one apprehends the vyakti as possessing<br>the generic attribute, cowness. But this argument that the significative<br>potency in a word signifies only the generic attribute, is rejected<br>by the Naiyāyikas. The śakti in the word cow does not, by itself,<br>signify only the generic attribute inasmuch as the generic attribute<br>is non-distinguishable from the individual. Moreover, it is also obvious<br>that the generic attribute can be apprehended only through the<br>apprehension of an individual. Also, when a man is told, bring a<br>cow, he does not bring the jati inherent in the cow, but fetches<br>the individual possessing the jati. Thus, the word cow helps him<br>to apprehend the cow in its totality in which the ideas of jāti,<br>the vyakti and the akrti (form) merge to give a composite concept<br>of the object. That is to say, the sakti of a word signifies all<br>the three, viz it suggests the form, denotes the individual, and connotes<br>the genus. Thus, say the Naiyāyikas, acceptance of a distinct pramāņa<br>like arthāpatti to apprehend an individual is only superfluous]<br>Reply: It is not so; because, when one says, bring a cow,<br>the activity of bringing would be possible only when the<br>significative potency is applied to the individual (since it is<br>impossible to fetch only the generic attribute to the exclusion<br>of the individual; nor any hearer ever proceeds to fetch the<br>generic attribute of the vyakti when he is called upon to<br>bring a cow). This is observed by the usage of elders.<br>Therefore, the argument that the significative potency of<br>a word connotes an object as determined by its generic attribute<br>(and not merely the generic attribute) has to be accepted.<br>And, this is apprehended by observing the usage of elders.<br>The madhyama vṛddha (ie the second elder who moves to<br>carry out the mandate) proceeds to the activity of fetching<br>a cow on hearing from the first elder person the sentence,<br>bring a cow, and following the instruction, he fetches the<br>cow. The boy (seated nearby, but is ignorant of the exact<br>denotations of the words in the sentence, but is desirous<br>of achieving proficiency in the science of etymology, observes<br>all this and arrives at the conclusion of the producibility of<br>knowledge by a proposition (jñānasya vākyajanyatvam), by<br>applying the method of affirmation and negation. Again, with<br>the help of āvāpa and udvāpa in sentences like bring the<br>horse, bind the cow, there arises in the inquisitive boy the<br>apprehension that the significative potency of the word cow<br>is apprehended in cow as determined by the adjunct cowness<br>and the significative potency of the word horse is apprehended<br>in the object horse as determined by the adjunct horseness. <br>[107. Vṛddha vyavahara or the usage of elders is one of the<br>means to understand the meaning of a word. When the first elder<br>commands the second elder to fetch a cow, the second elder goes<br>out and brings in a cow. The boy who observes all this, arrives<br>at the following syllogism: Every activity is preceded by an understanding<br>of what one is called upon to do; because it spurs one to activity;<br>even like my own activity; this is an activity; therefore, it must have<br>been preceded by an apprehension of what one is called upon to do<br>yā yā pravṛttih sā sā tatkartavyatā-jñānapūrvikā; pravṛttītvāt;<br>matpravrttivat; iyam pravṛttih; tasmāt tat kartavyatājñānapūrvikā. Thus<br>reflecting, the boy concludes that the act of bringing the cow is<br>the result of the knowledge that precedes the action. This knowledge<br>that prompted the second elder to the activity of bringing the cow<br>arises only on the hearing of the command from the first elder<br>because in the absence of such a command no activity was observed.<br>The boy comes to the above conclusion through the following syllogism:<br>gavānayanakartavyatājñānam gāmānayeti vākyaśravaņajanyam; vākya-<br>śravaņānanritabhāvitvāt; yo yadanantarabhāvī sah tajjanyah, yathā<br>mrdanantarabhāvī ghatah mrjjanyataḥ; pravṛttijanakajnānañca vākya-<br>śravanajanyam; tasmāt vākyajñānajan yam the knowledge that one is<br>called upon to fetch the cow is the result of the hearing of the sentence,<br>'bring a cow:' because of its dawning subsequent to the hearing of<br>the sentence; whichever is produced being preceded by something that<br>is produced by that, even as a jar which follows clay is the product<br>of clay, the knowledge that prompts one to activity is born of the<br>hearing of the sentence; therefore, inasmuch as the knowledge follows<br>the hearing of the sentence, the sentence produces the knowledge. Reasoning<br>thus, the boy comes to know that (a) the sentence from the first<br>elder produces the knowledge in the second elder that he is called<br>upon to do a certain activity and (b) that knowledge prompts him<br>to activity. To put it in other words, the sentence produces the knowledge,<br>and the knowledge prompts man to activity. But the boy has not<br>yet understood exactly the meaning of words like cow and horse.<br>Here by means of avāpa and udvāpa he comes to know the particular<br>significance of the corresponding words found in a sentence. Avāpa<br>is padāntara prakṣepah; ie the substitution of a fresh word in a<br>sentence, and udvāpa is the elimination of an already existing word<br>in a sentence. In the sentence gamānaya, the word go is substituted<br>by a fresh word aśva. Thus substituted the sentence reads, aśvamānaya.<br>The inquistive boy who is ignorant of the meanings of the words<br>in the sentence, observes the second person bringing a cow on hearing<br>from the first person gāmānaya. Though the boy concludes from<br>this that the proposition set forth by the first elder has given rise<br>to the cognition which prompted the second elder to fetch the cow,<br>he is unable to apprehend the exact meaning of the words gam<br>and ānaya in the sentence. Then he again hears the first elder commanding<br>the second elder: aśvamānaya. The boy observes the second elder<br>fetching the horse on hearing the sentence. The boy compares the<br>words in the above two sentences and observes the corresponding<br>activity of the second elder, and arrives at the conclusion that the<br>word ānāya which is common in both the sentences, indicates, the<br>activity of bringing and the words go and aśva indicate cow and<br>horse respectively]<br>Objection: Inasmuch as everywhere the purpose of the usage<br>of a sentence is to motivate one into activity, it cannot be<br>said that a sentence unaccompanied by any copula generates<br>the knowledge of significative potency in an object.<br>Reply: This is not so; because in statements like: In Kāñci<br>Tribhuvana Tilaka etc the meaning is understood through popular<br>usage. Also in statements like: a bee in a bloomed lotus<br>etc the meaning of the word madhukara is apprehended on<br>account of its contiguity to a well-known word (ie bloomed<br>lotus) even though the above statement does not motivate<br>one into activity.<br>[108. Now, the above explanation forces us to admit that only<br>those statements having copula in liṅ, lot and tavya pratyayas, ie<br>verbs with potential, imperative and mandatory suffixes, motivate one<br>to activity and thereby convey a meaning which inter alia means<br>that statements which do not motivate one to activity do not also<br>convey any meaning. This leads one to the conclusion that only<br>such statements as motivate one to an activity are authoritative sentences<br>and statements which are without any verb and consequently are<br>not capable of prompting one to activity, are unauthoritative statements,<br>lacking the force or power to convey any meaning. To refute this<br>contention, Annambhaṭṭa quotes the statement: in Kāñci.... as an example.<br>Statements like these only restate an already established fact and<br>are not made with a view to motivate one to activity; still they<br>convey meanings. So, the argument that only such statements as<br>are having copula in liṅ, lot and tavya suffixes are authoritative<br>sentences, cannot be sustained. Also, Annambhaṭṭa does not assert<br>that the usage by the elders - vṛddha vyavahāra, alone is the means<br>to apprehend the meaning of a word. He also quotes another example:<br>A bee in a bloomed lotus. Here, the meaning of a hitherto unknown<br>word madhukara is apprehended by virtue of its contiguity to the<br>well-known word vikasita padma. The various means of apprehending<br>the significative potency of a word are: grammar, comparison, dictionary,<br>statement of a trustworthy person; usage of elders, supplementary<br>statements, commentary, and contiguity of a well-known word—śaktigrāho<br>vyākaraṇa-upamāna-kośa-āptavākyat- vyavahārātaśca; vākyasya śeṣāt-<br>vivṛteḥ-vadanti sānnidhyataḥ siddhapadasya vṛddhāḥ]<br>Implication is the relation between a word and the meaning<br>of that word. (By the term vrtti, both śakti and lakṣaṇā,<br>are meant, as lakṣaṇā too, like śakti, is a vrtti in śabda).<br>Implication is the relation with the direct meaning of a word.<br>In statements like: A cowherd's hamlet on the Ganges etc<br>we get the implied meaning of on the bank of the Ganges<br>on account of the relation of the bank with the stream,<br>signified by the word, Gaṅgā. Therefore, in the above instance,<br>the significative potency cannot be said to indicate the bank<br>also. In words like, saindhava, etc several śaktis are to be<br>presumed on account of the absence of any relation between<br>salt and horse. <br>[109. In the example of gaṅgāyām ghoṣaḥ, pravāha is taken as<br>the vācyārtha and the tīra as lakṣyārtha. Similarly, why not interpret<br>the two meanings of saindhava, viz, salt and horse, as vācyārtha<br>and lakṣyārtha respectively? This doubt is cleared by Annambhaṭṭa<br>in his Dīpikā in which he says: ‘On account of the absence of<br>relationship, ie on account of non-existence of any relation between<br>the two meanings of the word saindhava. Only that which has connection<br>or relation with vācyārtha can be lakṣyārtha. In the example of gaṅgāyām<br>ghoṣaḥ, bank is connected to current which is the vācyārtha of gaṅgā;<br>but it cannot be said that the two meanings of saindhava, viz, salt<br>and horse, are related to each other in any sense. Here both the<br>meanings must be admitted as vācyārtha only, viz, the word saindhava<br>has two śaktis, one signifying salt and another signifying horse. So<br>too are the words like Hari having many śaktis]<br>Implication is threefold: jahallakṣaṇā, ajahallakṣaṇā and<br>jahadajahallakṣaṇā. Jahallakṣaṇā is that where the primary<br>meaning is wholly abandoned and a new one is substituted;<br>eg the statement: Cots cry out. Ajahallakṣaṇā is that where<br>the word conveys something more, while retaining the sense<br>of the primary meaning, as is found in the statement:<br>Umbrella-holders are going. Jahadajahallakṣaṇā is that where<br>only a part of the statement is abandoned, as is found in<br>the Vedic dictum: Thou art That. <br>[110. The statement: mañcaḥ krośanti, when taken in its primary<br>meaning, means, Cots are crying which is absurd. This absurdity can<br>be avoided only through the implied meaning which the statement<br>yields through its association with the primary meaning. By implication,<br>the above statement means: The child on the cot is crying. Here,<br>the word mañca implies the meaning of the child by virtue of its<br>relation with the cot. The relation here between the cot and the<br>child is ādhārādheyabhāva. ie the relation between the supported and<br>the support. Thus, in order to get a sensible meaning, the primary<br>meaning has to be abandoned entirely and substituted by a totally<br>different meaning. This method of discarding the primary meaning<br>and substituting it by implied meaning is known as jahallakṣaṇā.<br>In the example: chatriṇaḥ gacchanti - men holding umbrellas are<br>going, the primary meaning is in the doership consisting in the activity<br>of going in those who are holding umbrellas. Since the above sentence<br>is employed with the intention of including also those who accompany<br>them without umbrellas, the primary meaning implies also men without<br>umbrellas. Thus, when a sentence conveys something more than what<br>its bare primary meaning conveys, it is known as ajahallakṣaṇā.<br>The third variety is jahadajahallakṣaṇā. The great Vedic dictum:<br>Tattvamasi is the example cited. The word, Tat in the dictum signifies<br>Consciousness conditioned by Māyā (illusory power) and takes on<br>such attributes as omniscience, omnipotence etc. The word tvam signifies<br>the Consciousness conditioned by the limitations of antahkarana or<br>avidyā and takes on such attributes as partial omniscience, partial<br>omnipotence etc. Here, the attributes ascribed to Consciousness signified<br>by Tat and Tvam are mutually contradictory and arise consequent<br>upon the conditioning adjuncts of Māyā in the case of Tat and<br>avidyā or antahkarana in the case of Tvam. The real sense of<br>the dictum Tattvamasi is that Tat is the same as Tvam but this<br>sense of Oneness cannot be had as long as the above terms are<br>qualified by contradictory attributes arising on account of the respective<br>limiting adjuncts. When these limiting adjuncts are removed, the dictum<br>conveys its real sense, viz, oneness of Consciousness signified by<br>Tat and Tvam in Tattvamasi. This method of abandoning a certain<br>part while retaining another part is known as jahadajahallakşaņā or<br>bhāgatyāga lakşaņā]<br>Gaunī also is a form of lakṣaṇā on account of its relation<br>with the qualities in the thing which is apprehended through<br>implication as in the statement, agnirmanavakaḥ - the student<br>is fire. Vyañjanā or the suggestive mode of expression also<br>is included in śakti (expression) and lakṣaṇā (implication);<br>because it springs from śabdaśakti, ie vyañjanā depends on<br>the force or potency of a word having multiple meanings,<br>and from arthaśakti, ie from the force of the meaning of<br>the word, and it is obtained through inference etc.<br>[111. It cannot be said that the intended sense in statements<br>like agnirmāņavakah is obtained by lakşaņā since there exists no<br>direct relation between the student and the fire. The statement really<br>intends to convey the sense that the student is as brilliant and powerful<br>as fire itself. Here, the relation is between the qualities found in<br>the student and the qualities inherent in fire. This meaning cannot<br>be had through lakşaņā as here there is no śakyasambandha. Therefore,<br>the vetti through which this intended meaning is got must be a distinct<br>one, and is known as gauni vrtti. But the Naiyāyikas argue that<br>gauņi vṛtti is only a form of laksana since here too śakyasambandha<br>can be established, though indirectly. The quality found in the student<br>is similar to the quality inherent in fire. Thus, Naiyāyikas say, there<br>is no need to accept gauņi vrttii as distinct from lakşaņā to explain<br>the above.<br>The rhetoricians recognise yet another kind of vetti, viz, vyañjanā<br>or the suggestive mode of expression. This is resorted to when the<br>intended sense of the word cannot be had either through abhidhā<br>or through lakşaņā. For example, the statement, gangāyām ghosah<br>may also be interpreted to mean the cowherd's hamlet is cool and<br>sacred. This meaning cannot be had either through abhidha or through<br>lakşaņā. So, the Alamkarikas say that the mode through which this<br>suggested meaning is obtained is known as vyañjanā]<br>The non-apprehension of the intended sense is the cause<br>of implication. Tātparya or intention is an utterance with a<br>desire to convey the intended sense. And the apprehension<br>of the intention is the cause of the knowledge of the meaning<br>of the sentence because of its lending itself to many meanings.<br>The context helps in apprehending the intended sense. In<br>statements like door, the word close should be supplied (to<br>get at the intended sense of the speaker when an incomplete<br>sentence is uttered).<br>Objection: The purpose of word is to have the knowledge<br>of the meaning. Without knowing the meaning it is not possible<br>to supply the particular word conveying that meaning. Therefore,<br>it is but proper to supply the meaning itself (and not the<br>word).<br>Reply: It is not so; because, only such meaning as is produced<br>by the corresponding word is the cause of understanding the<br>meaning of the sentence. Otherwise, in statements like pot<br>is karmatvam, and bringing is kṛti also there will be the<br>possibility of śabdajñāna.<br>[112. Annambhatta says in his Dīpikā that implication becomes<br>necessary only when the true intention of a person is not revealed<br>by the primary meaning of the sentence. It is a common experience<br>that the knowledge of the intention of the speaker is essential to<br>have a correct understanding of the sentence. That is why tatparya<br>jñāna is said to be one of the causal requisites that help the<br>comprehension of the correct sense of the statement. For example,<br>the statement: kākebhyo dadhi rakṣyatām - protect the curd from crows,<br>implies that all birds and creatures must be kept off the curd. Here<br>the word kākebhyo is only a suggestion implying every other bird<br>and creature from which the curd is to be protected. Thus in order<br>to get at the exact intention of the speaker one has to go in for<br>the implied meaning. In certain cases the intended sense springs from<br>the context in which the sentence is used, eg the statement,<br>saindhavamānaya. The word saindhava signifies two objects, viz, salt<br>and horse. Therefore, when one is told to bring saindhava one will<br>be at a loss to understand whether the speaker intends horse or<br>salt. That can be ascertained only if one knows the intention of<br>the speaker. In some places, the intention of the speaker is understood<br>by supplying a particular word conveying the appropriate meaning<br>to make the meaning sensible, eg when one says, dvāram - door;<br>another word pidhehi - close must be supplied to get at the exact<br>intention of the speaker; because, the meaning of the sentence springs<br>from consistency among the words in the sentence. If the word pidhedhi<br>is not supplied to dvāram to complete the sentence, there will be<br>the absence of consistency between the words in the sentence and<br>consequently the intention of the speaker to close the door will<br>not be clear]<br>In words like pankaja etc the mode used is yogarūḍhi,<br>the mode in which both the etymological and customary<br>significances are partly retained. Yoga or etymological significance<br>is the power of several roots or component parts of a word.<br>Rūḍhi or customary significance is the power or force of<br>the composite whole. In order to get at the customary meaning<br>of pankaja as lotus, the power of composite whole is used;<br>otherwise, the word pankaja may also mean lily etc. <br>[113. The etymological meaning of pankaja is anything born of<br>mud. So, if we take pankaja in its yoga mode, ie in the purely<br>etymological sense, then it may mean many things that are born<br>in mud, viz, lotus, lily, etc. Therefore, to restrict the meaning of<br>pankaja to lotus only, the rüdhi mode must be applied to the word,<br>that is to say, the word pankaja signifies, through unbroken tradition,<br>lotus only and nothing else]<br>The followers of Prabhākara opine that the significative potency<br>is in the meaning which has coherent connection with other<br>meanings (itarānvita). The followers of Gautama are of the<br>opinion that inasmuch as the construction of the meaning<br>of the words is understood as the meaning of the sentence<br>through the sentence in which the words are used in a coherent<br>order, the significative potency should not be imagined in<br>the construction also.<br>[114. According to Naiyāyikas a vākya is a combination of padas,<br>and vākyārtha is obtained by the coherent construction of the meanings<br>of words in the sentence. They say that the coherent construction<br>of the meanings which are already got from the words is vākyārtha,<br>that is to say, first the words give only unconnected meanings, and<br>it is only after this, that the connection among the meanings is<br>understood. Thus the meaning of the sentence as a whole is merely<br>the synthetic construction or anvaya, of the meanings of the constituent<br>words of the sentence. This is known as abhihitānvaya vāda. Thus,<br>when we hear the sentence, gām ānaya, first, the meanings of the<br>constituent words are comprehended one after another. Thus, by gam<br>is meant the cow as the object and by ānaya is meant the activity<br>of bringing. This is first apprehended. Then, by synthesizing the meanings<br>of these words, the synthetic meaning of the whole sentence, viz,<br>gokarmakam ānayanam - the activity of bringing of an object known<br>as cow or, ānayana karma gauh, cow is the object of the activity<br>of fetching, takes place. Thus there is no need to accept significative<br>potency in construction also. Annambhatta further tells us that the<br>different meanings expressed successively by the words are synthesised<br>by means of memory. Thus when we come to the last word, the<br>memory of the meanings of the preceding words helps us in synthesising<br>them with the meaning of the last word, thus enabling us to construe<br>a coherent meaning of the whole sentence. This theory is accepted<br>by the Vedanta and the Bhatta Mimāṁsā systems also. Yet another<br>theory known as anvitābhidhāna vāda is propounded by the followers<br>of Prabhākara, which theory holds that every word in a sentence<br>conveys the meaning connected with the meanings of other words<br>in the sentence in a syntactic manner. Thus when a bāla hears<br>the sentence, gām ānaya, the syntactic meaning of the whole sentence<br>is first apprehended by the observance of vṛddha vyavahāra. It is<br>only after this that the bāla tries to know the meanings of particular<br>words through the process of āvāpa and udvāpa.<br>A sentence means an action; every sentence revolves round a<br>copula, without which a sentence never conveys any meaning. Thus<br>a verb, either mandatory or prohibitory, is the central unit of a<br>sentence. All the other words give a sensible meaning only when<br>they are related to the copula. Thus in the sentence, gām ānaya,<br>go has to be understood as the object of the verb ānaya. Only<br>thus connected to the verb, the sentence conveys the sense, bring<br>a cow. Thus, in a sentence, first there is anvaya with one another<br>in a syntactic manner and then the (abhidhāna) expression of the<br>meaning of the whole sentence occurs]
        - **Item 88**:
          - **verse_number**: `88`
          - **original_text**: 2. Verbal expectancy, compatibility, and juxtaposition:<br>आकाङ्क्षा योग्यता संनिधिश्च वाक्यार्थज्ञानहेतुः पदस्य पदान्तर-<br>व्यतिरेकप्रयुक्तान्वयाननुभावकत्वम् आकाङ्क्षा । अर्थाबाधो<br>योग्यता । पदानामविलम्बेनोच्चारणं संनिधिः ॥ २ ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 2. Verbal expectancy, compatibility, and<br>juxtaposition are the causes of the knowledge of the<br>meaning of a sentence. Verbal expectancy consists in<br>the inability of a word to convey the meaning of a<br>sentence due to the absence of some other word.<br>Compatibility is non-contradiction of the sense.<br>Juxtaposition is the utterance of words in quick succession.
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: आकाङ्गेति ॥ आकाङ्क्षादिज्ञानमित्यर्थः । अन्यथा आकाङ्क्षादिभ्रमाच्छाब्दभ्रमो न स्यात् ।<br>आकाङ्क्षां लक्षयति - पदस्येति ॥ योग्यतालक्षणमाह - अर्थेति ॥ संनिधिलक्षणमाह - पदानामिति ॥ अविलम्बेन पदार्थोपस्थितिः संनिधिः । उच्चारणं तु तदुपयोगितयोक्तम् ।
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>The word verbal expectancy in the Text has to be interpreted to mean, the knowledge of verbal expectancy etc. Otherwise, (if it is merely said that verbal expectancy is the cause of apprehension of verbal testimony) there can occur no such knowledge inasmuch as an erroneous apprehension of verbal testimony can arise in a place where one has an erroneous notion of verbal expectancy. (So, it is the knowledge of verbal expectancy that is the cause of the apprehension of verbal testimony. Similarly, the knowledge of compatibility etc is the cause of the apprehension of verbal testimony). The Text defines compatibility; artha.... Juxtaposition is defined by the Text: padānām.... Juxtaposition is the continuous apprehension of the meaning of words. Utterance is inserted in the Text to indicate its utility in grasping the meaning of the objects indicated by the word. <br>[115. In this passage Annambhatta enumerates, defines and explains<br>the three conditions recognised in the Nyaya School of thought as<br>necessary for the understanding of a statement — Śabda bodha.]
        - **Item 89**:
          - **verse_number**: `89`
          - **original_text**: 3. Verbal Expectancy:<br>आकाङ्क्षादिरहितं वाक्यमप्रमाणम् । यथा गौरश्वः पुरुषो हस्तीति न प्रमाणमाकाङ्‌क्षाविरहात् । अग्निना सिञ्चेदिति न प्रमाणं योग्यताविरहात् । प्रहरे प्रहरेऽसहोच्चारितानि गामानयेत्यादिपदानि न प्रमाणं सांनिध्याभावात् ॥ ३ ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 3. A sentence devoid of verbal expectancy etc is<br>unauthoritative; eg the words: a cow, a horse, a man,<br>an elephant, etc are not authoritative as they are devoid<br>of verbal expectancy. The sentence sprinkle with fire<br>is not authoritative owing to the absence of compatibility.<br>Words like bring.....a.....cow etc pronounced at long<br>intervals are not authoritative owing to want of<br>Juxtaposition.
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: गौरश्व इति ॥ घटकर्मत्वमित्यप्यनाकाङ्क्षोदाहरणं द्रष्टव्यम् ।
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>Simple statements like a cow, a horse etc and (such<br>sentences) as jar being an accusative (ghaṭaḥ karmatvam) etc<br>are examples of sentences that lack verbal expectancy in them. <br>[116. Above is given, in brief, the accessories necessary to arrive<br>at valid verbal testimony. These accessories are three as mentioned<br>above, with tatparyajñāna, ie the knowledge of the intention of the<br>speaker, as the fourth one. What is ākānksā? To put it simply,<br>it is the eagerness that is observed in one to know the remaining<br>part of the sentence when one part of the sentence is known. Annambhatta<br>says in his Dīpikā that ākānksā consists in the inability of a word<br>to convey complete meaning of the sentence in the absence of some<br>other word. The Text gives an example of a simple statement like<br>ghatah. When one says just ghatah, the curiosity to know further<br>details like: what about the jar arises. This is satisfied only when<br>we supply words like bring etc. This alone completes the sentence.<br>This desire to know is ākānksā. Well, it is seen that even without<br>a desire to know, one may understand the sense of a statement.<br>Therefore, ākāñksā, signifies an awareness of the relation between<br>words in a sentence, eg in the sentence, bring a jar, the term<br>bring is not able to produce, without the term Jar, the awareness<br>of the relation between those two words in the sentence. This inability<br>in the word to produce anubhava or apprehension between the two<br>words in the absence of the other word is ākānksā as the Dipikā<br>puts it. As an example, Annambhatta cites collection of some terms<br>such as, a cow, a horse, a man, an elephant etc. These do not<br>bring about any understanding of the sense of the statement inasmuch<br>as there is the absence of ākānksā in them.<br>The second accessory is yogyata, compatibility. A sentence is<br>said to possess yogyata with another when the intended sense of<br>the sentence conveyed by the two words are not inconsistent in<br>any way. The sentence: agninā sincet lacks compatibility on account<br>of the inconsistency found in the sentence. The action of sprinkling<br>is inconsistent with the notion of fire, and the construction of a<br>syntactic meaning from such a sentence as this which lacks compatibility<br>is impossible. Therefore, yogyata is one of the accessories necessary<br>for rendering the sentence authoritative.<br>The third accessory is sannidhi, juxtaposition. A sentence becomes<br>authoritative only when the words are uttered in quick succession.<br>If one says gām and utters anaya after a long interval, the impression<br>made by each word does not remain fresh till the combination of<br>the words in the sentence is effected. Thus, to ensure the unbroken<br>apprehension of the meanings of all the words, they should be uttered<br>in quick succession. Secondly, if the gap between the utterances<br>of words is too long, they cannot form a sentence either. Therefore,<br>juxtaposition of words by means of utterance in quick succession<br>is one of the essential accessories to render a sentence authoritative.<br>The fourth is tātparyajñāna, the knowledge of the intention of<br>the speaker. In the statement kākebhyo dadhi rakṣyatām, (protect the<br>curd from the crows), the intended meaning of the speaker is to<br>protect the curd from all animals and birds from being eaten and<br>not merely from crows. Thus, a knowledge of the intention of the<br>speaker is one of the accessories to render a sentence authoritative]
        - **Item 90**:
          - **verse_number**: `90`
          - **original_text**: 4. Sentence:<br>वाक्यं द्विविधम् । दैदिकं लौकिकं च । वैदिकमीश्वरोक्तत्वा-<br>त्सर्वमेव प्रमाणम् । लौकिकं त्वाप्तोक्तं प्रमाणम् ।<br>अन्यदप्रमाणम् ॥ ४ ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 4. Sentence is of two kinds: sacred and secular. The sacred being the pronouncements of God, are all authoritative. But the secular pronouncements made by<br>trustworthy persons only are authoritative; all pronouncements made by others are unauthoritative.
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: वाक्यं विभजते - वाक्यमिति ।। वैदिकस्य विशेषमाह वैदिकमीश्वरोक्तत्वादिति ॥ ननु वेदस्यानादित्वात्कथमीश्वरोक्तत्वमिति चेत् न । 'वेदः पौरुषेयः वाक्यसमूहत्वात् भारतादिवत्' इत्यनुमानेन पौरुषेयत्वसिद्धेः । न च स्मर्यमाणकर्तृकत्वमुपाधिः, गौतमादिभिः शिष्यपरम्परया वेदेऽपि सकर्तृकत्वस्मरणेन साधनव्यापकत्वात् । 'तस्मात्तेपानात्त्रयो वेदा अजायन्त' इति श्रुतेश्च । ननु वर्णा नित्याः, स एवायं गकार इति प्रत्यभिज्ञाबलात् । तथा च कथं वेदस्यानित्यत्वमिति चेत् न, 'उत्पन्नो गकारो विनष्टो गकार' इत्यादिप्रतीत्या वर्णानामनित्यत्वात्, 'सोऽयं गकार' इति प्रत्यभिज्ञायाः 'सेयं दीपज्वाला' इतिवत्साजात्यावलम्बनत्वात्, वर्णानां नित्यत्वेऽप्यानुपूर्वीविशिष्टवाक्यस्यानित्यत्वाच्च । तस्मादीश्वरोक्ता वेदाः । मन्वादिस्मृतीनामाचाराणां च वेदमूलकतया प्रामाण्यम् । स्मृतिमूलवाक्यानामिदानीमनध्ययनात्तन्मूलभूता काचिच्छारवोत्सन्नेति कल्प्यते । ननु पठ्यमानवेदवाक्योत्सादनस्य कल्पयितुमशक्यतया विप्रकीर्णवादस्यायुक्तत्वान्नित्यानुमेयो वेदो मूलमिति चेत् न, तथा सति कदापि वर्णानामानुपूर्वीज्ञानासंभवेन बोधकत्वासंभवात् ।
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>The Text divides a sentence; vākyam.... The distinguishing quality of sacred pronouncements is explained: vaidikam....<br>Objection: The Vedas are beginningless. How can it then be said that they are pronounced by Iśvara?<br>Reply: It is not so; the Vedas are pauruşeya because they are a collection of sentences, even like the Mahābhārata etc. This inference establishes that the Vedas are pauruşeya. Nor can it be argued that this inference is vitiated with the defect of adventitious condition (upādhi) consisting in the remembrance of the authorship of the work; because, here, the reason is pervasive of the remembrance of the authorship, since the authorship of the Vedas was known, as it was remembered by sages like Gautama through the tradition of unbroken tutelage from teacher to disciple. Srutis too declare: The three Vedas are born of Isvara through His austerity. (Tasmat tapānāt trayah vedah ajāyanta)<br>Objection: Letters (varnah) are eternal owing to the<br>reminiscence (pratyabhijñā): "this letter ga is verily that." This<br>being so, how can the Vedas be non-eternal? (ie the Vedas<br>being spoken by God, have a beginning and are thus<br>impermanent. But the letters being eternal, how can the Vedas<br>be non-eternal, since the Vedas are only collection of sentences?)<br>Reply: It is not so; the non-eternality of letters is proved<br>by the cognition: this letter ga is produced: this letter ga is<br>destroyed. The reminiscence this letter ga is verily that letter<br>ga arises depending on the common quality in them. <br>[117. To elaborate: gatva is common in all ga-letters due to which<br>a notion of identity between them rises like the cognition: This<br>is that flame, arising on account of the similarity of subsequent flames<br>with the preceding ones, which similarity depends on the sameness<br>of class. Also, even if the letters are accepted as eternal, the Vedas<br>cannot be considered eternal on account of the non-eternality of<br>the sentences in which is found a particular order of arrangement<br>of letters and words. Therefore, the Vedas are the utterances of<br>Iśvara.<br>The Vedas are the collection of sentences that are set in a<br>particular order. This setting of sentences in a particular order as<br>found in the Vedas must have been effected by some one. As no<br>human origin is possible in the case of works of such transcendental<br>wisdom they must be the work of God Himself. Thus the Vedas<br>are the compositions of Iśvara. They have thus a beginning and all<br>get destroyed at the end of a kalpa. Vedas are, thus, non-eternal]<br>The canons of Manu and others, and other canons prescribing<br>code of conduct etc are authoritative because they derive<br>their authority from the Vedas, they being their sources.<br>Whenever certain Vedic sources are untraceable, it should<br>be presumed that those branches of the Vedas which form<br>the sources for the smrti texts, but which are not traceable<br>now, have been lost owing to the discontinuance of the study<br>of those sections in the modern times.<br>Objection: But it is impossible to presume the diappearance<br>of the Vedic sections which are being recited generation after<br>generation. Also, it is inappropriate to presume that the Vedic<br>passages are scattered everywhere and consequently they are<br>untraceable. Therefore, it must be admitted that the untraceable<br>Vedic sources which form the authority for the smrti texts<br>by Manu and others, were also inferred by them as they<br>are being inferred so by us now.<br>Reply: It is not so; further, even if your argument is admitted,<br>it cannot be sustained inasmuch as it is very difficult to<br>recognise the particular order of arrangement of letters used<br>in the Vedas, and the Vedas, in which the recognition of<br>this order of letters and words is lacking, cannot produce<br>any valid verbal cognition. (Therefore, Manu and others did<br>not infer the Vedic Sources but were conversant with them<br>which are lost to the present generation).<br>[118. The Naiyāyikas hold that the Vedas are pauruşeya. They<br>establish this through the inference given in the Dipika:<br>(i) Veda is the paksa; pauruşeyatva is the sādhya; vākyasamūhatvāt<br>is the sādhana; Mahābhāratādi is the example. The Vedas are<br>the collections of sentences; therefore, he argues, the authorship also<br>must be found in them keeping in view the maxim that wherever<br>there is sādhana there must also be the sādhya. Therefore, the Vedas<br>are pauruşeya, ie utterances by Iśvara. This argument is refuted by<br>the Mīmāṃsakas. They argue that the above anumāna is defective<br>as it is vitiated by the smaryamāṇakartṛkatva upādhi; here,<br>smaryamāṇakartṛkatva is the upādhi as it is pervasive of the sādhya<br>while being non-pervasive of the sādhana. To explain: wherever there<br>is pauruşeyatva, as in Mahābhārata etc there is also<br>smaryamāṇakartṛkatva; because, Vedavyāsa is remembered as the author<br>of the Mahābhārata. This is sādhya vyāpakatā. Wherever there is<br>vākyasamūhatva there is not found smaryamāṇakartṛkatva; this is sādhana<br>avyāpakatā. Though both vākyasamūhatva and smaryamāṇakartṛkatva<br>are found in Mahābhārata etc in the Vedas is found only vākyasamūhatva<br>and not smaryamāṇakartṛkatva. Thus, in the Vedas is found sādhya<br>vyāpakatā as well as sādhana avyāpakatā. So, the above inference<br>is vitiated by the smaryamāṇakartṛkatva upādhi, and, as such, the<br>inferential knowledge arrrived at by such an inference cannot be<br>valid. Naiyāyikas refute this by pointing out that smaryamāṇakartṛkatva<br>is found in the Vedas also since sages like Gautama and others<br>remembered that the Vedas were the compositions of Iśvara.<br>(ii) The doctrine of the eternality of letters put forward by the<br>Mīmāṃsakas is also refuted by the Naiyāyikas. The reminiscence this<br>letter ga is verily that on the basis of which the theory of the eternality<br>of letters is established, takes place on account of the knowledge<br>of similarity of class. Letter ga is infinite, it being pronounced by<br>an infinite number of persons an infinite number of times, and each<br>letter is destroyed the very next moment of its utterance. But the<br>genus gatva that is the class attribute of all gakāra-s that are uttered<br>and destroyed, is the same. It is on account of the recognition of<br>this common class gatva that the reminiscence this letter ga is verily<br>that letter ga arises. In illustration, the Dīpikā mentions Dīpajvālā.<br>There is nothing like a continuous flame; nor does one ever take<br>a second dip in the same current of water. But the perception of<br>unbroken continuity of similar class of objects gives birth to the<br>knowledge of a continuous flame in case of a flame, and river,<br>in case of flowing current of water.<br>(iii) Naiyāyikas hold that smṛtis are authoritative since they are<br>based on the Vedic Texts. Then what about those Smṛtis whose<br>sources are not found in the Vedas? The Dīpikā answers this by<br>saying that the Vedic Texts which provide authority to the Smṛtis<br>but are untraceable now, existed no doubt, but in course of time<br>some branches of the Vedas became obsolete owing to the discontinuance<br>of their study by the later generations. These forgotten sections of<br>the Vedas might well have been the sources of the Smṛtis for which<br>no Vedic authority can be cited now. Therefore, Smṛtis do not become<br>unauthoritative simply because Vedic sources are not traceable now.<br>In such cases their existence must be inferred as having existed<br>and known to Manu etc when they composed their Smṛti Texts.<br>Of the three types of Smṛtis, viz, (a) that which follows the Śruti,<br>śrutyanusārī, (b) that for which Vedic Source is untraceable, and<br>(c) that which is contrary to the Śruti, Śruti-viruddha, the first two<br>varieties are authoritative; the first variety being in assonance with<br>the Vedic Text; and the second variety deriving its authority from<br>inferred Vedic Text. The third variety is rejected forthwith as<br>unauthoritative as it is contrary to the teachings of the Vedas]
        - **Item 91**:
          - **verse_number**: `91`
          - **original_text**: 5. Meaning of a sentence:<br>वाक्यार्थज्ञानं शाब्दज्ञानम् । तत्करणं शब्दः ॥ ५ ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 5. The apprehension arising from valid testimony<br>is the knowledge of the meaning of a sentence. Its<br>special cause is the word.
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: ननु एतानि पदानि स्मारितार्थसंसर्गवन्ति आकाङ्क्षादिमत्पदकदम्बकत्वात् मद्वाक्यवत्<br>इत्यनुमानादेव संसर्गज्ञानसंभवाच्छब्दो न प्रमाणान्तरमिति चेन्न । अनुमित्यपेक्षया<br>विलक्षणस्य शाब्दज्ञानस्य 'शब्दात्प्रत्येमि' इत्यनुव्यवसायसाक्षिकस्य सर्वसंमतत्वात् ।<br>अन्वर्थापत्तिरपि प्रमाणान्तरमस्ति 'पीनो देवदत्तो दिवा न भुङ्क्ते' इति दृष्टे श्रुते<br>त्रा पीनत्वान्यथानुपपत्त्या रात्रिभोजनमर्थापत्त्या कल्प्यत इति चेन्न । 'देवदत्तो रात्रौ<br>भुङ्क्ते दिवाऽभुञ्जनत्वे सति पीनत्वात्' इत्यनुमानेनैव रात्रिभोजनस्य सिद्धत्वात् ।<br>शते पञ्चाशदिति सम्भवोऽप्यनुमानमेव । 'इह बटे यक्षस्तिष्ठति' इत्यैतिह्यमपि<br>'अज्ञातम्लवक्तृकशब्द एव । चेष्टापि शब्दानुमानद्वारा व्यवहारहेतुरिति न प्रमाणान्तरम् ।<br>तस्मात्प्रत्यक्षानुमानोपमानशब्दाश्चत्वार्येव प्रमाणानि ॥
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>Objection: Verbal testimony is not an independent means<br>of valid knowledge because of the possibility of having the<br>knowledge of the syntactic meaning of the whole sentence<br>through inference: these words connect the meaning remembered<br>by them; because they possess the group of words having necessary<br>accessories like verbal expectancy; even like my own sentence.<br>(Therefore, śabda is not a distinct prāmāna)<br>Reply: It is not so; because, the apperception<br>(anuvyavasāyajñāna: "I know this by verbal testimony" differs<br>from the apperception: "that is imparted by an inference,"<br>like, "I know this by inference." This dissimilarity between<br>the two apperceptions is a universally recognised fact. (Therefore,<br>we have to admit Śabda as a distinct pramāna). <br>[119. Vaiśeṣikas admit only two pramāṇas, viz, pratyakṣa and<br>anumāna. They include śabdapramāṇa in anumānapramāṇa. Annambhaṭṭa<br>gives the argument of the Vaiśeṣikas in his Dīpikā in the form of<br>an anumāna in which the words or the meanings of words in a<br>sentence form the pakṣa svasmāritārthasamsargavanti - the connection<br>of meanings remembered through those words - forms the sādhya;<br>ākāṅkṣādimatpadakadambakatvat - on account of group of words possessing<br>such accessories as verbal expectancy etc forms the reason; madvākyavat<br>- even like my sentence - forms the example. Putting forward this<br>inference, the Vaiśeṣikas argue that while the valid verbal knowledge<br>can be had through inference, taking recourse to a distinct pramāṇa<br>like śabda to arrive at a valid verbal knowledge is superfluous. But<br>the Naiyāyikas refute this argument by pointing out dissimilar<br>apperceptions that arise respectively through inference and word. If<br>the argument of the Vaiśeṣikas that śābdajñāna also arises through<br>anumāna, is accepted, then the apperception that arises through anumāna<br>must be: imamartham anuminomi - I know this by inference. But<br>this is dissimilar to the apperception that rises among men, for,<br>it is observed that one gets the notion, śabdat amumartham pratyemi<br>this I know through word, whenever śābdajñānā is gained. Therefore,<br>śabda is a distinct means of proof.]<br>Some (ie the Mīmāmsakas) admit of still another proof<br>known as presumption (arthāpatti); eg when the knowledge,<br>fat Devadatta does not eat by daytime is obtained either by<br>actual sight (drste) or by heresay from another person (śrute),<br>the presumption that Devadatta eats by night arises, as otherwise,<br>his remaining fat without eating cannot be explained. But<br>this conclusion (arrived at by presumption) about Devadatta's<br>eating by night can be had through inference itself, like,<br>Devadatta eats by night; for, he is fat though he does not<br>eat by day. (As such, there is no need for admitting a distinct<br>pramāņa like Arthāpatti).<br>Similarly sambhava (inclusion, which is a distinct proof<br>accepted by the Paurāņikas) through which the knowledge<br>of fifty arises when one has the knowledge of hundred, is<br>also a form of inference. (The knowledge of the part when<br>one has the knowledge of the whole, can be had by inference).<br>Aitihya (Tradition, which is accepted as a distinct proof by<br>the Paurāņikas) through which the knowledge, A spirit dwells<br>in this tree is gained from the proposition of some unknown<br>person, is included in Śabda pramāņa since here also vākya<br>is the source of such a belief. Ceṣṭā (gesture, yet another<br>proof admitted by the Tāntrikas) also is not an independent<br>proof; because, it becomes the cause of communication with<br>another only by means of śabda arising from the inference<br>(that is arrived at by observing the gestures).<br>Thus proofs are only four, viz, Perception, Inference,<br>Analogy and Word.<br>[120. Before concluding the section on buddhi, Annambhatta deals<br>with the problem of epistemology. Every school of thought has to<br>deal with the moot epistemological question: How do we know? The<br>Naiyāyikas answer this question by maintaining four pramāņās as the<br>instrumental causes of valid knowledge, viz, pratyaksa, anumāna,<br>upamāna and śabda. Having dealt with these in his Text and Dīpikā<br>in some detail, Annambhatta now proceeds to discuss the validity<br>and non-validity of knowledge gained by these causal apparatuses.<br>How can it be said that the knowledge got through any of the<br>above mentioned four pramāņās is not a misapprehension? It is observed<br>sometimes that, on further verification, the knowledge hitherto held<br>to be valid proves to be erroneous. Then, how to ensure that a<br>certain knowledge is yathārtha or ayathārtha? This problem is discussed<br>by Annambhatta in his Dīpikā. He first states the view-point of the<br>Mīmāmsakas in this connection before coming to refute their arguments<br>and to establish the Naiyāyika view-point]

              - **commentary**: ज्ञानानां तद्धति तत्प्रकारकत्वं स्वतोग्राह्यं परतो वेति विचार्यते । तत्र विप्रतिपत्तिः<br>ज्ञानप्रामाण्यं तदप्रामाण्याग्राहकयावज्ज्ञानग्राहकसामग्रीग्राह्यं न वा इति । अत्र<br>विधिकोटिः स्वतस्त्वम् । निषेधकोटिः परतस्त्वम् । अनुमानादिग्राह्यत्वेन सिद्धसाधनवारणाय<br>यावदिति । 'इदं ज्ञानमप्रमा' इति ज्ञानेन प्रामाण्याग्रहाद्बाधवारणाय<br>अप्रामाण्याग्राहकेति ॥ इदं ज्ञानमप्रमा<br>इत्यनुव्यवसायनिष्ठप्रामाण्यग्राहकस्यापि<br>तस्मिन्ग्राह्यप्रामाण्या-<br>अप्रामाण्याग्राहकत्वाभावात्स्वतस्त्वं न स्यादतस्तदिति ।<br>श्रयेऽप्रामाण्याग्राहिकेत्यर्थः । उदाहृतस्थले व्यवसायाप्रामाण्यग्राहकस्याप्यनुव्यवसाये<br>तद्गाहकत्वात्स्वतस्त्वसिद्धिः । ननु स्वत एव प्रामाण्यं गृह्यते, 'घटमहं जानामि'<br>इत्यनुव्यवसायेन घटघटत्वयोरिव तत्सम्बन्धस्यापि विषयीकरणात् व्यवसायरूप<br>प्रत्यासत्तेस्तुल्यत्वात् । पुरोवर्तिनि प्रकारसम्बन्धस्यैव प्रमात्वपदार्थत्वादिति चेत् न ।<br>स्वतःप्रामाण्यग्रहे 'जलज्ञानं प्रमा न वा' इत्यनभ्यासदशायां प्रमात्वसंशयो न स्यात् ।<br>अनुव्यवसायेन प्रामाण्यस्य निश्चितत्वात् । तस्मात्स्वतोग्राह्यत्वाभावात्परतो ग्राह्यत्वमेव ।<br>तथाहि । प्रथमं जलज्ञानानन्तरं प्रवृत्तौ सत्यां जललाभे सति पूर्वोत्पन्नं जलज्ञानं<br>प्रमा सफलप्रवृत्तिजनकत्वात् यन्नैवं तन्नैवं यथा अप्रमा इति व्यतिरेकिणा प्रमात्वं<br>निश्चीयते । द्वितीयादिज्ञानेषु पूर्वज्ञानदृष्टान्तेन तत्सजातीयत्वलिङ्गेनान्वयव्यतिरेकिणापि<br>गृह्यते । प्रमाया गुणजन्यत्वमुत्पत्तौ परतस्त्वम् । प्रमासाधारणकारणं गुणः,<br>अप्रमासाधारणकारणं दोषः । तत्र प्रत्यक्षे विशेषणवद्विशेष्यसंनिकर्षो गुणः । अनुमितौ<br>व्यापकवति व्याप्यज्ञानम् । उपमितौ यथार्थसादृश्यज्ञानम् । शाब्दज्ञाने यथार्थयोग्यताज्ञानम् ।<br>इत्याद्यूहनीयम् । पुरोवर्तिनि प्रकाराभावस्यानुव्यवसायेनानुपस्थितत्वादप्रमात्वं परत एव<br>गृह्यते । पित्तादिदोषजन्यत्वमुत्पत्तौ<br>परतस्त्वम् । ननु सर्वेषां ज्ञानानां<br>यथार्थत्वादयथार्थज्ञानमेव नास्तीति । न च 'शुक्ताविदं रजतम्' इति<br>ज्ञानात्प्रवृत्तिदर्शनादन्यथाख्यातिसिद्धिरिति वाच्यम् । रजतस्मृतिपुरोवर्तिज्ञानाभ्यामेव<br>प्रवृत्तिसंभवात् । स्वतन्त्रोपस्थितेष्टभेदाग्रहस्यैव सर्वत्र प्रवर्तकत्वेन 'नेदं रजतम्' इत्यादौ<br>अतिप्रसङ्गाभावादिति चेत् न । सत्यरजतस्थले पुरोवर्तिविशेष्यकरजतत्वप्रकारकज्ञानस्य<br>लाघवेन प्रवृत्तिजनकतया शुक्तावपि रजतार्थि प्रवृत्तिजनकत्वेन विशिष्टज्ञानस्यैव कल्पनात् ।
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>Here, a doubt arises whether the validity and non-validity<br>of valid and non-valid cognitions are self-proved (swatah) or<br>proved by some other means (paratah). Mīmāmsakas accept<br>that validity is self-proved. They accept that validity becomes<br>self-proved when it becomes the object of the knowledge<br>brought about by all causal materials (by all the respective<br>materials in respect of all types of knowledge) which do<br>not produce non-validity in the knowledge which is the<br>substratum of validity (tadaprāmānyā-grāhaka-yāvajjñānagrāhaka-<br>samagrigrahyam).<br>If yāvat (all) is removed, then the body of sādhya will<br>be as follows: Being the object of the knowledge brought about<br>by causal materials which do not produce non-validity in the<br>knowledge which is the substratum of validity. Accordingly, in<br>the inference: This knowledge is valid; because of its being<br>capable of making the action fruitful, validity becomes the<br>object of the knowledge that is brought about by the materials<br>(sāmagrīs) which produce knowledge which is the substratum<br>of validity. As such it becomes, as it were, that the Mīmāmsakas<br>have proved only what is already known and is established<br>by the Naiyāyikas in respect of the above inference and have<br>failed to prove that apperception (anuvyavasāya which they<br>hold as capable of apprehending validity) and all other inference<br>etc., apprehend validity, thus proving that validity is self-proved<br>(svatogrāhya). When yāvat is included, the siddha sadhana<br>(ie proving what is already known) defect is averted and<br>apperception and all inference also become included in the<br>sādhya.<br>In the knowledge, This knowledge is non-valid - idam<br>jñānam aprama, the non-validity (aprāmānya) of the cognition<br>is apprehended. Here is the absence of apprehension of validity.<br>So, here occurs the defect of badha (self-contradiction). To<br>avert this defect, it is said, that which does not apprehend<br>non-validity (aprāmānya agrāhaka).<br>The validity found in the apperception (anuvyavasāyajñāna):<br>This knowledge is non-valid is not agrāhaka to aprāmānya.<br>To avert this defect, tat (ie tasmin - in the knowledge which<br>is the substratum of validity) is brought together with<br>aprāmānyāgrāhaka etc. The meaning is: validity consists in<br>the knowledge that does not apprehend the non-validity of<br>the knowledge that apprehends validity. In the above examples,<br>though there is the apprehension of non-validity in vyavasāya,<br>there is the non-apprehension of non-validity in apperception.<br>Thus, it is proved that the validity of knowledge is apprehended<br>by the same body of causal apparatus that apprehends knowledge.<br>Mīmāmsakas argue further thus: The Naiyāyikas favour the<br>view that this and jarness are apprehended as viśeşya and<br>prakāra respectively in the apperception that apprehends: this<br>is a jar. In other words, due to close contiguity of knowledge,<br>those two are apprehended as such. On the strength of the<br>same close contiguity of knowledge, why not they accept<br>that the relation of the attribute too is apprehended in the<br>object in view?<br>In this connection, the following doubt arises: First, one<br>has the knowledge: This is water. Next arises the apperception<br>I know this water. In this apperception, water, its genus jalatva,<br>and the relation between water and its genus -- all these<br>three are objects, and all these are valid apprehensions. Here,<br>their validity is proved by the same samagrī that produces<br>knowledge (ie svatogrāhyam). Then, where is the occasion<br>for entertaining a doubt whether the validity is proved by<br>the same samagri that brings about the knowledge or by<br>some other means (like inference etc.)? In a place where<br>the validity of a knowledge stands self-proved, there can be<br>no room for any doubt whether the knowledge of water is<br>valid or not. But a doubt has been raised. So, it has to<br>be admitted that validity is not apprehended by apperception.<br>Therefore, it is not self-proved but proved by other means<br>(paratogrāhyam). For example, first one has the knowledge<br>of water, then he goes to take that water and finds the<br>water. Here, the knowledge of water is valid because, this<br>knowledge of water produces an action which in turn produces<br>the fruit (of getting the water to drink etc). A negative example<br>(vyatireka drstānta) for the same is also given: That which<br>is not like this, is not like this, ie where there is no motivation<br>for an action that produces a fruit, there is also no validity.<br>In this way, the validity of the first knowledge is determined.<br>The second and third knowledges are also determined to<br>be valid on the strength of the example of the first knowledge.<br>Thus, it is proved that the validity of knowledge is proved<br>by inference (ie parataḥ prāmānya).<br>Now a doubt arises as to what causes the production<br>of validity in knowledge and what causes the apprehension<br>of validity in knowledge. Here, the discussion is as to whether<br>validity also is caused by the causal apparatus that produces<br>knowledge (jñāna utpādaka sāmagri) or by the causal apparatus<br>other than the causal apparatus that produces knowledge?<br>Here Naiyāyikas conclude that the validity of perception is<br>born of guna. In this case, the contact of the eye with<br>the object capable of producing valid perception is the guna.<br>If it is non-validity it is born of defect (dosajanya). In inferential<br>cognition the knowledge of the vyapaka (which is greater<br>in extent) as existing in a place where there is vyāpya (which<br>is less in extent) is the guna. In analytical knowledge (upamiti)<br>the knowledge of resemblance is the guna. In verbal knowledge<br>(sābdajñāna) the knowledge of proper compatibility etc is the<br>guna. Thus, the causal apparatus of the validity of a knowledge<br>being different from the causal apparatus bringing about a<br>knowledge (jñānasāmagri), it ought to be admitted that the<br>production of the validity of knowledge (jñānaprāmānya utpatti)<br>and the apprehension of validity (jñapti) are both caused by<br>other sources (paratah). A white conch appears as yellow<br>owing to the defect of jaundice attacking the eye. Here,<br>this erroneous cognition is due to the defect caused by jaundice<br>(pittadosa).<br>The followers of Prabhakara say that all knowledges are<br>valid (pramā) and that there is nothing like erroneous knowledge<br>(bhrama). The cognition, this is silver is not one knowledge;<br>in fact, here we have two knowledges: the knowledge of<br>this is born of direct perception (pratyaksa-janya) and the<br>knowledge of silver is born of remembrance (smarana-janya).<br>One proceeds to pick up silver only if there is the absence<br>of the knowledge, the thing before me is not silver. Therefore,<br>there is no need to have an attributive knowledge (viśişta<br>jñāna) to motivate one to an activity of picking up the silver<br>piece. So, there is nothing like erroneous knowledge. But<br>the Naiyāyikas refute this contention saying that the man<br>who is prompted to pick up the silver proceeds to the place<br>thinking, this is silver. Here, the knowledge, this is silver<br>must be an attributive knowledge. If he has the knowledge<br>of a thing as silver which in reality is not silver, such a<br>knowledge is erroneous knowledge. Thus the contention that<br>there is no erroneous knoweldge is not correct.
        - **Item 93**:
          - **verse_number**: `93`
          - **original_text**: SECTION IX<br>ERRONEOUS APPREHENSION<br>6. Erroneous apprehension: three-fold:<br>अयथार्थानुभवस्त्रिविधः संशयविपर्ययतर्कभेदात् ॥ ६ ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 6. Erroneous apprehension is of three types: doubt,<br>misapprehension, and false assumption.
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: अयथार्थानुभवं विभजते<br>त्रैविध्यविरोधः<br>अयथार्थ इति ॥ स्वप्नस्य मानसविपर्ययरूपत्वान्न
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>The Text divides erroneous knowledge: Ayathārtha.... The<br>experience that one has in a dream is of the nature of<br>mental misapprehension of facts; thus, there can be no objection<br>to the classification of erroneous experience into three<br>varieties. <br>[121. An objection is raised that the classification of erroneous<br>apprehension is inexhaustive inasmuch as dream also could be included<br>in the category of erroneous apprehension since it is a state other<br>than recollection and never a true reproduction of any experience<br>in the waking state; nor can it be identified with memory cognition;<br>as such it falls under anubhava, and is quite different from the above three<br>varieties. Why not, then, include dream as the fourth one?<br>Annambhatta rejects this argument though he has no objection<br>to dream being known as a form of erroneous anubhava; he calls<br>it mānasa (viparyaya) ie it is an illusion of the mind (not an illusion<br>of the outer senses as sensory illusion is due to some sense-affectation)]
        - **Item 94**:
          - **verse_number**: `94`
          - **original_text**: 7. Doubt:<br>एकस्मिन्धर्मिणि विरुद्धनानाधर्मवैशिष्ट्यावगाहि ज्ञानं संशयः ।<br>यथा स्थाणुर्वा पुरुषो वेति ॥ ७ ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 7. The cognition that apprehends several mutually<br>contradictory attributes in the same attributive thing is<br>doubt, like: Is it a post or a man?
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: संशयलक्षणमाह - एकेति ॥ घटपटाविति समूहालम्बनेऽतिव्याप्तिवारणाय एकेति ।<br>'घटो द्रव्यम्' इत्यादावतिव्याप्तिवारणाय विरुद्धेति । घटत्वविरुद्धपटत्ववान् इत्यत्र<br>अतिव्याप्तिवारणाय नानेति ।
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>The characteristic of doubt is being explained: Ekasmin....<br>The term, Ekasmin dharmini is added to the above definition<br>of doubt to avert the defect of overapplicability in the knowledge<br>apprehending a group of things like: Jar and cloth etc. The<br>word viruddha is added to avert overapplicability in statements<br>like: A jar is an object etc. The term nānā (different) is<br>added to avert overapplicability in the knowledge: This possesses<br>jarness which is contradictory to the attribute cloth-ness<br>(patatvaviriuddhaghatatvavān). By the use of the word Viruddha<br>(contradictory) viruddhatvam (contradiction) is apprehended.<br>In other words, jarness with viruddhatvam becomes apprehended.<br>As such ekasmin dharmiņi viruddha dharmavatvam (ie<br>apprehending contradictory attributes in the same substratum)<br>applies to the knowledge: Patatvaviruddha ghatatvavān, where<br>patatva viruddhatvam is apprehended in the jar as prakāra.<br>By adding nānā the defect of overapplicability is avoided;<br>because, both viruddhatvam and jarness are not different to<br>each other inasmuch as they reside in the same substratum<br>and do not reside in different substrata.
        - **Item 95**:
          - **verse_number**: `95`
          - **original_text**: 8. Viparyaya:<br>मिथ्याज्ञानं विपर्ययः । यथा शुक्तौ इदं रजतमिति ॥ ८ ॥
          - **commentaries**:
            - **Item 1**:
              - **author**: English Paraphrase
              - **commentary**: 8. Misapprehension is erroneous knowledge, like<br>the knowledge: That is silver in a piece of oyster shell.
            - **Item 2**:
              - **author**: Sanskrit Commentary
              - **commentary**: विपर्ययलक्षणमाह मिथ्येति ॥ तदभाववति तत्प्रकारकनिर्णय इत्यर्थः ।
            - **Item 3**:
              - **author**: English Translation
              - **commentary**: Dīpikā:<br>The Text defines misapprehension: mithyā....<br>Misapprehension is the cognition of an attribute in a thing<br>in which such an attribute does not exist in reality. When<br>one has the knowledge of silver in an oyster shell, it is<br>called an erroneous knowledge because the attribute of silverness<br>does not exist in the piece of oyster shell.
        - **Item 96**:
